He was not sure who hired her, as he “was not involved in the \[Company’s\] affairs”.[^174] He did not know her salary when she joined the Company, because he “wasn’t even paying attention to those matters.”[^175] When asked if he knew of her last drawn salary, he replied, “I do not know about her salary at all”.[^176] Indeed, he was not even sure if she reported to TSM:[^177]

> Q:    And who does \[Quah\] report to?

> A:    I **think** it was \[TSM\].

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

113    LEC’s awareness of the Company’s financial affairs was no better. In fact, he testified that he was not involved in the Company’s financial matters.[^178] He knew that its customers paid in cash or by way of cheques.[^179] However, he did not know if _cash_ cheques were an acceptable mode of payment because he was “not in charge of this”.[^180] He said that Quah kept track of whether customers paid. But, he was not sure if TSM did so as well. In any event, he said that he “did not really care about this” as he believed that TSM was in charge:[^181]

> Q:    And how then does \[the Company\] keep track of whether clients have paid for the diesel?

> A:    \[Quah\] will track.

> Q:    And apart from \[Quah\] tracking, are there any other forms of checks?

> A:     **No. I do not know** if \[TSM\] is managing this. **I believe** that \[TSM\] is in charge.

> Q:    Okay.

> **A:**      **So I did not really care about this.**

> \[emphasis in bold\]

114    LEC said that he never asked about the bank statements for the Company’s Account because he was “not involved in this.”[^182] Neither did he keep track of these statements. He only discovered there were issues with the Company’s accounts _after_ TSM told him that Quah’s replacement was not able to balance the accounts.[^183]

115    LEC’s limited awareness of the Company’s financial affairs and the significant amount of trust he reposed in Quah were clear when he was referred to the 2014 audit verification.[^184] This had stated that the Company’s records showed that he owed the Company $61,231.15 as at 30 June 2014 – although it was not disputed that LEC had never borrowed any money from the Company (and so did not owe the sum stated).[^185] That said, he had appended his signature in the “Confirmation Reply” section of the letter to (apparently) confirm the correctness of the sum he allegedly owed the Company. In examination-in-chief, he testified that Quah had (only) told him that the document was for filing the accounts when she gave it to him to sign.[^186] He repeated this evidence when cross-examined. However, he also testified that he would simply just sign off “whatever documents for the filing of accounts that were given to \[him\]”:[^187]

> Q:    Okay Mr Lim, can you now pick up P16? P16 is a letter dated 7th of August 2014 that was addressed to you. Okay. If you look at the section with the title “Confirmation Reply”, do you see just beside it, there seems to be a signature or an initial? Or to be fair to you, there appears to be some handwritten note.

> A:    Yes, it’s a handwritten note.

> Q:    Do you know who wrote it?

> A:    I do not know.

> Q:    So, when you signed off on this confirmation reply, that handwritten note wasn’t there?

> A:    I did not pay attention to this. When I signed these documents, I seldom pay attention to all these.

> …

> Q:    Okay but, Mr Lim, earlier in your evidence, you said that ‘Mabel”…gave you this document to sign, and she said that it was to file the accounts, right?

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **So you knew when you signed this, it was for purposes of filing the accounts?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **Okay so, it’s actually quite an important document, right?**

> **A:**      **I do not know about this.**

> Q:    Okay, Mr Lim, you are a director of the \[C\]ompany.

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    Since 1974, right?

> A:    I think so. When the \[C\]ompany started, I was already a director.

> **Q:**      **Okay, Mr Lim. So, my point really is that, if you weren’t sure about what this document is, you shouldn’t have signed it, right?**

> **A:**      **Because all along, we trusted our clerk, \[Quah\].**

> Q:    Okay, but even before \[Quah\] came in, you would have had another accountant, right?

> A:    Another clerk, yes. Yes, we had.

> **Q:**      **Okay, and also when documents that were prepared for the filing of accounts, you would also just sign it?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> Q:    When you signed this document marked P16, if you see somewhere in the middle of the page, there are 2 signatures, right?

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **Okay, were the 2 signatures there when you signed off?**

> **A:**      **I did not pay attention.**

> **Q:**      **Okay, Mr Lim. I’m going to put it to you, okay, that whatever documents for the filing of accounts that were given to you, you simply just signed off.**

> **A:**      **I agree.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

116    LEC testified similarly when referred to the 2014 audit verification issued to TSM. This had stated that the Company’s records showed that TSM owed the Company $100,435.08 as at 30 June 2014. According to LEC, “\[t\]here was totally no explanation” of this document when Quah gave it to him to sign.[^188] He also said that he did not know the purpose of this document when he signed it. Neither did he know that that TSM owed the Company $100,435.08 as at 30 June 2014. Notwithstanding all of these, he still appended his signature as “Director” to inform TSM of this debt owed to the Company.

117    LEC’s evidence in respect of the FY2014 Report[^189] was also telling.[^190] In examination-in-chief, he had testified that he did not receive any director’s fees or shareholder dividends between 2009 and 2014. According to LEC, he had only received director’s fees in 2017 and 2018.[^191] In cross-examination, his attention was then drawn to the last page of the report – which stated that $48,000 had been paid out for “Directors’ fee” for the financial year ending on 30 June 2014. In this regard, he testified that he had not received _any_ part of this sum. He also said that he did not know if TSM or NES had.[^192] Indeed, he testified that he had simply signed on the report without looking at it because he had left it to the auditors to do their work:[^193]

> Q:    Okay. What I’m trying to ask is that, earlier you said you didn’t receive any director’s fees from 2009 to 2014, but there audited accounts shows that director’s fees were paid out. So, did you receive any part of this 48,000?

> A:    No.

> **Q:**      **Did any other director receive this sum?**

> **A:**      **I do not know about this.**

> **Q:**      **Okay, so you are saying that some director’s fees could have been paid out but not paid out to you?**

> **A:**      **These accounts is done by \[the Audit Firm\], I’m not sure.**

> **Q:**      **So again, Mr Lim, you are saying that you signed off on these audited accounts…but left it to the auditors to do that work, is it?**

> **A:**      **Yes, I did not look at it.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

118    LEC testified that he knew that TSM loaned money to the Company to tide it over financial difficulties. However, he did not know when this practice started. He was also not sure how much money TSM had lent the Company and the number of occasions he had done so. In addition, he was not sure if the Company had already repaid TSM. He further said that he did not know if TSM was still lending money to the Company because he “\[did\] not care about the accounts”.[^194]

119    In cross-examination, LEC was referred to the Inquiry Letters. His testimony showed that he was not even aware of who the Company’s lawyers were. He even asked Counsel to clarify if the Company’s lawyers were in fact its lawyers, before going on to say that he had never seen these letters:[^195]

> Q:    Okay Mr Lim, we have \[the Inquiry Letter addressed to Tan Kok Chuan\]. Okay, and it’s under the letterhead of “Lai Mun Onn and Company”.

> **A:**      **What is this company? I do not know.**

> Q:    Okay, so you do not know who Lai Mun Onn and Company is?

> **A:**      **I do not know.**

> …

> Q:    Okay, now, if you look at \[the Inquiry Letter addressed to Teo Tai Joo\], under Lai Mun Onn and Co’s letterhead.

> **A:**      **Is the---is Lai Mun Onn our lawyers?**

> Q:    Mr Lim, I think you have to tell us that.

> **A:**      **I do not know much about…these letters.**

> Q:    Okay…if you look through \[the Inquiry Letters addressed to the Clients\], you are saying that you have never seen these letters before?

> **A:**      **I have never seen \[them\].**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

120    It was also LEC’s evidence that he had never seen the Demand Letters. He also did not know if they were ever sent to Quah.[^196]

#### NES’s limited awareness of Company’s affairs

121    The evidence also showed that NES’s involvement in, and knowledge of, the Company’s affairs were very limited (notwithstanding its small size). Like LEC, NES’s demeanour as he testified showed that he knew very little about the Company’s affairs. Indeed, when asked about his responsibilities as a director, he replied: to “take care of the oil”, “take care of the oil pumping area, and to clean up the place”.[^197]

122    NES did not know when Quah joined the Company and who hired her. He also said that he did not oversee Quah’s work. He was not aware of her job scope as well.[^198]

123    NES was aware that customers paid in cash or by way of cheques. However, he did not know who stipulated these modes of payment. He also did not know what Quah was supposed to do with the cash and cheques she collected from customers.[^199] When asked how the Company kept track of whether its customers paid, he replied, “I do not know. All were handled by \[Quah\]”.[^200] He also could not recall if he ever saw the bank statements for the Company’s Account.[^201] When asked about his involvement in the Company’s financial affairs, he responded: “I do not know about those”.[^202]

124    NES said that he knew that TSM loaned the Company money, but he explained that this was because TSM had said he did. When asked why TSM did so, NES replied “\[TSM is\] in charge of the money. He is the boss”. However, he did not know how much money TSM had loaned the Company.[^203] He also did not know if the Company had already repaid TSM.

125    NES testified that TSM was in charge of managing the Company’s “internal affairs”. That said, when asked what he meant by “internal affairs”, he simply responded that TSM was “in charge of the internal jobs”. It was not at all clear what “internal affairs’ or “internal jobs” meant. NES was not able to elaborate further.

126    In cross-examination, NES was referred to the Inquiry Letters and the Demand Letters.. Like LEC, he testified that he:[^204]

> (a)     had never seen these letters before;

> (b)     was not aware if the Demand Letters were ever sent to Quah; and

> (c)     did not know that M/s Lai Mun Onn & Co were the Company’s lawyers.

#### LEC & NES relied on TSM to handle Company’s affairs

127    LEC’s and NES’s limited involvement in the Company’s affairs were supported by their consistent references to TSM as their “boss”[^205] – although they were _all_ directors. In cross-examination, LEC was asked why he referred to TSM as his “boss” when they were _both_ directors. He agreed that he did so because, as far as he was concerned, TSM “was handling the \[Company’s\] affairs on behalf of all the directors”:[^206]

> Q:    Okay, Mr Lim, you and \[TSM\] are both directors of the company, right?

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **Okay, why do you refer to him as your boss?**

> **A:**      **Usually, I would address him as so, because he is the one who handles everything.**

> **Q:**      **So, am I right to say that as far as you are concerned, \[TSM\] was handling the affairs of the company on behalf of all the directors?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> Q:    Okay, and for you, you were just concerned about whether the company was making money or not?

> A:    Yah, it should be like this, **as long as I have money to take. That’s all.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

128    In closing submissions, Counsel argued that there was “no doubt in \[LEC’s and NES’s\] minds that TSM exercised complete control over \[Quah’s\] duties as an employee”.[^207] It was also contended that “it was evident that \[Quah\] reported to TSM, and TSM did supervise her”.[^208] I did not find these submissions persuasive. The evidence showed that LEC and NES knew very little about the Company’s affairs. They also did not know much about Quah’s and TSM’s duties in the Company. LEC, in particular, was not sure if Quah reported to TSM and if TSM was the one in charge of keeping track of whether customers paid. Indeed, LEC candidly admitted that he was only concerned with whether the Company made money.[^209] LEC and NES did not therefore appear to be in any position to comment on whether TSM exercised “complete control” or supervision over Quah’s duties. They effectively relied on TSM to handle the Company’s affairs (but this did not in itself mean that TSM did _in fact_ handle these affairs).

#### TSM relied on Quah to handle Company’s accounts

129    TSM testified that Quah was “in charge of everything” relating to the Company’s accounts between 2009 and 2014:[^210]

> Q:    …Who else, if anyone, worked with \[Quah\] on \[the Company’s\] accounts between 2009 and 2014?

> **A:**      **No, she take over everything and she was in charge of everything.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

130    When asked as to whom Quah reported to in the Company, TSM said that she “_should_ report to \[him\]”. According to TSM, Quah did “\[i\]nitially \[report to him\] for a few years.” He also oversaw her work initially “for quite some years”. But, he “just let her do her work” “after 7 to 8 years” after he underwent surgery for heart problems. He also thought that she “was quite good after working for \[him\] for a few years”.[^211]

#### Quah did not explain audit verifications to Directors

131    The Directors all testified[^212] that they had never borrowed money from the Company. Quah did not challenge this aspect of their evidence. Indeed, she agreed in cross-examination that they had never borrowed money from the Company.[^213]

132    Quah was then referred to the audit verifications issued to LEC and NES in 2013 and 2014.[^214] These had stated that LEC and NES owed money to the Company (and so suggested that they had indeed borrowed money from the Company). But, she disagreed that these verifications contradicted her evidence that the Directors had never borrowed money from the Company. Instead, she claimed – for the _firs_t time – that the figures in these letters referred to monies “accumulated from the past”.[^215] In re-examination, she testified that the sum stated in the verification issued to NES in 2013 related to money that NES owed the Company before 2009. According to Quah, NES had not returned money to the Company after selling a vehicle the Company had bought him. She claimed that TSM wanted to use the verification to prove that NES had not repaid the Company. This also appeared to be her position on the verifications issued to LEC in 2013 and 2014, as well as the verification issued to NES in 2014.[^216]

133    I rejected Quah’s fresh claims. They were not raised when the Directors testified. She also failed to explain the sums on the verifications issued to TSM in 2013 and 2014.

134    Since the Directors never borrowed money from the Company, it would have made no sense for them to sign the verifications _if_ they knew that these letters:

> (a)     stated that they owed money to the Company;[^217] and

> (b)     required their confirmation that they did indeed owe large sums of money to the Company (ranging from $43,357.40 to $100,435.08).

135    Quah must therefore have failed to explain the contents of these verifications to the Directors when she asked the Directors to sign them. This was in fact the Directors’ evidence as well.[^218] They all testified that they did not understand the substantive contents of the verifications that Quah had given them to sign.[^219] They also said that they did not owe the Company the sums stated in these letters.[^220]

136    In particular, LEC testified that Quah did not tell him that the verifications issued to him stated that he owed money to the Company. According to LEC, Quah had only told him that they were needed for filing the Company’s accounts.[^221] This was also TSM’s evidence in relation to the verification issued to him in 2014:[^222]

> Q:    And what did \[Quah\] explain to you about this document when she asked you to sign it?

> **A:**      **She only said that it was required by Heng Lee Seng for the accounts and then we would have to sign.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

137    NES’s evidence in cross-examination was also relevant. This was because he had also signed on the verifications issued to LEC to confirm that LEC did indeed owe the sums stated to the Company. I set out the relevant portions of NES’s evidence:[^223]

> Q:    So, can you recall who gave you \[the audit verification issued to LEC in 2013\] to sign?

> A:    \[Quah\].

> **Q:**      **And when you signed this document, were you told \[by Quah\] what it was for?**

> **A:**      **No.**

> **Q:**      **And did you ask her?**

> **A:**      **No. When she took it to me and asked \[me\] to sign, \[I\] signed.**

> …

> Q:    Okay. Okay, my instructions are that when \[Quah\] gave this document to you to sign, she did tell you that it states that there is an amount owing by \[LEC\] to the company.

> **A:**      **No, she did not say. She did not say.**

> Q:    Okay, can you now look at \[the audit verification issued to LEC in 2014\]?...Okay do you see your signature appearing in the middle of the document.

> A:    Yes.

> …

> Q:    Okay. So, who gave you this document to sign?

> A:    \[Quah\].

> …

> **Q:**      **Can you recall what \[Quah\] told you regarding this document?**

> **A:**      **She did not say.**

> **Q:**      **So you simply just signed it?**

> **A:**      **Yes. Yes, when she took it to me, I would sign.**

> **Q:**      **Did you ever ask why you had to sign these 2 documents?**

> **A:**      **No, I did not.**

> Q:    And do you know who prepared these documents?

> A:    She brought it to me and I signed, how would I know who prepared the\[m\]?

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

138    TSM’s evidence in cross-examination was relevant as well. Like NES, he had signed the verification issued to LEC in 2014. When asked if Quah had explained the purpose of this document when he signed it, TSM testified as follows:[^224]

> A:    Usually when \[Quah\] asked us to sign something, if it’s not for tax purposes it will be what the government requires or for the accounts then we would sign for her. **We do not know anything about English at all so whatever she said we will sign.**

> Q:    But, Mr Tan, like I said, you’ve been a director since 1974. You are signing documents without finding out what the content of the document is?

> **A:**      **She just said “You sign this, I will not harm you.” So she asked me to sign, I will sign. This is what is required by the government and required by income tax, so we would sign.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

139    NES similarly testified that he had signed the verifications issued to him because Quah had asked him to.[^225]

#### Directors trusted Quah when signing audit verifications

140    The significant amount of trust reposed in Quah by the Directors was evident from the audit verifications. The evidence showed that the Directors all signed these letters without question – although:[^226]

> (a)     their proficiency in the English language (the language used in these letters) was very limited;

> (b)     they did not know how to use ACCPAC (and so were not able to independently access the Company’s accounting records to verify the figures stated in these letters); and

> (c)     Quah did not explain their contents and implications.

141    This was also supported by LEC’s and TSM’s testimony. In cross-examination, LEC was asked why he signed the verification issued to him in 2014 when he was not sure of its import. He explained that he did so because he trusted Quah:[^227]

> Q:    Okay, Mr Lim. So, my point really is that, if you weren’t sure about what this document is, you shouldn’t have signed it, right?

> **A:**      **Because all along, we trusted our clerk, \[Quah\].**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

142    TSM also explained that he did not ask Quah about the verification issued to him in 2013 because he trusted her:[^228]

> Q:    Did you ask \[Quah\] about this document which you had to sign?

> A:    I did not ask her.

> Q:    Why not?

> **A:**      **Because when I employed her to do this, I would trust her.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

143    The amount of trust that TSM reposed in Quah was especially clear when he was asked about the audit verification issued to EDS in 2013.[^229] It was TSM’s evidence that Quah had written the contents of the letter. She had told him that EDS “owed \[the Company\] money for too long and…she wanted to demand payment."[^230] He was then asked if he therefore accepted that EDS owed the Company $26,309.98 as at 30 June 2013. He testified as follows:[^231]

> **A:**      **\[It was Quah\] who told me this, of course I believe my employee.**

> Q:    Okay. So if you didn’t agree with it, you wouldn’t have signed it right?

> **A:**      **I trusted her. Whatever amount she said, I would sign. If I do not trust her, I wouldn’t have employed her.**

> Q:    Mr Tan, in fact, I put it to you that you had signed it because you knew that that was the amount that EDS Enterprise owed the company.

> **A:**      **I do not know how much was owing by the company. \[Quah\] prepared this so I have to trust her. She’s my employee. I have to trust her.**

> Q:    Mr Tan, I put it to you that you also knew how much EDS Enterprise owed the company because you were keeping track of customer payments every month.

> A:    But if she cancel it, I would have thought that it was repaid…so I would just look at this piece of paper by her because if she was to cancel the 3 months payment and change it to 1 month, I also wouldn’t have known.

> Q:    Okay, Mr Tan, on this point I would put it to you that you would have known know much EDS Enterprise has paid to the company because you were keeping track of payments from customers.

> A:    I disagree.

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

#### Discussion between Directors & Quah before Quah’s resignation

144    In examination-in-chief, LEC testified that TSM had called for a discussion in the Office before Quah’s resignation in June 2014 (the “June 2014 Discussion”). This was because the business was not doing well. According to LEC, “every time when the business \[was\] not good, when we did not earn any money, we \[would\] be asked to come and discuss”. Quah and the Directors were present at this discussion. During the discussion, LEC asked why the Company was not making money. Quah then said “I don’t want to do anymore”, and told the Directors that she would resign. LEC then told Quah that if she really wanted to resign, she should write a letter (as he had told her “many times already” on previous occasions). According to LEC, Quah did not give any reasons for wanting to resign. TSM also “did not say much” at this discussion. No reasons were identified during this discussion as to why the Company was not making any money.[^232] It was LEC’s evidence that this discussion did not yield any conclusion. In cross-examination, LEC disagreed that the June 2014 Discussion never happened.[^233]

145    In a similar vein, NES testified that there was a meeting in the Office before Quah resigned in June 2014.. Quah and the Directors were present at this meeting, where the Company’s financial performance was discussed. During the meeting, Quah “said that there was no money”. She also said that “she did not want to do anymore”, to which LEC then asked her to write a letter if she really wanted to resign. According to NES, Quah did not give any reasons for wanting to resign. He also said that nothing much else was discussed at this meeting.[^234] NES’s evidence was not challenged in cross-examination.

146    TSM also testified that Quah would frequently say, “If you don’t trust me, I will resign”[^235] when the Directors asked her why the Company was not making money. Recalling the June 2014 Discussion, he said that LEC had told her to write a letter if she really wanted to resign. TSM’s evidence was not challenged in cross-examination.

147    I found that the June 2014 Discussion _did_ happen. During this discussion, Quah was asked why the Company was not making money. She responded by saying that she did not want to continue working at the Company, and did not explain why. LEC then told her that she should write a letter if she really did want to resign. This explained Quah’s resignation letter, dated 20 June 2014.[^236]

### Totality of evidence showed that Quah committed offences

148    Against the backdrop of my preliminary findings, I found that the totality of the evidence showed “inevitably and inexorably” that Quah dishonestly misappropriated the Entrusted Monies. I now elaborate.

#### Inherent deficiencies in Quah’s Compilation

149    To support her case, Quah prepared spreadsheets and produced them during her examination-in-chief (**D13A**; collectively, “the Compilation”). She said she prepared the Compilation to prove that she did not take the Entrusted Monies.[^237] It was her evidence that the Compilation made it easier to see how she withdrew the “cash cheques \[that she\] deposited into \[her three personal bank accounts\], to be given to \[TSM\]”.[^238] Quah had obtained the details listed in her Compilation from the bank statements of these three accounts.[^239] These details comprised:

> (a)     the account numbers;

> (b)     various sums ranging from $500 to $15,000, that Quah had withdrawn from each of her three accounts[^240]; and

> (c)     the dates and months of these withdrawals.

150    According to Quah, the withdrawals listed in her Compilation all pertained to the cash cheques she had collected from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex, which she had encashed by depositing into her personal accounts. It was her case that the Compilation showed that she withdrew a total of $451,400 from her accounts between 2 January 2010 and 16 August 2014. She claimed that she handed this entire sum to TSM on different occasions, and that TSM would record the sums given on each occasion in his Cash Record Book.[^241] That said, she agreed in cross-examination that her Compilation was deficient in the following aspects:[^242]

> (a)     it related only to her explanation for what happened to _some_ (and not all)[^243] of the cash cheques she had collected from EDS; Win Tran; and Time-Flex.

> (b)     it contained _no_ record of the payments she had collected from:

>> (i)       Tan Kok Chuan;

>> (ii)       Teo Tai Joo;

>> (iii)       Ku Aik Beng; and

>> (iv)       Sin Koon Seng Enterprise.

> (c)     it did not explain the difference of $468,421.69, between the:[^244]

>> (i)       total sum she had admitted to collecting from the Clients ($919,821.69, as reflected in the charges); and

>> (ii)       total sum of $451,400 stated in the Compilation.

> (d)     the withdrawals she listed in the Compilation did not correlate to the amounts stated on the cash cheques she deposited into her personal accounts. As Quah testified: “\[I\]f I deposited $10,000 of cash cheques into the account, it \[did\] not mean that I would withdraw $10,000…Sometimes \[it\] will be more, sometimes \[it\] will be less”.[^245]

151    In itself, the Compilation also did not show that Quah did _in fact_ hand a total of $451,400 to TSM.[^246] It was but a summation of selected withdrawals that Quah had made from her personal accounts. It did not therefore advance her case very much. Indeed, her cross-examination on the Compilation undermined her case severely.

#### Quah kept monies from cash cheques she encashed

152    In cross-examination, Quah agreed that her stance on the cash cheques she had collected from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex comprised only four steps:[^247]

<table align="left" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="Judg-2-tblr" frame="all" pgwide="1"><colgroup><col width="11.46%"> <col width="88.54%"> </colgroup><tbody><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Step 1</b></p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">She collected these cash cheques.</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Step 2</b></p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">She deposited <b>all</b> of these cash cheques into her three personal bank accounts, as reflected in her Compilation.</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Step 3</b></p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">She withdrew <b>all</b> of the monies corresponding to these cash cheques from her three personal bank accounts.</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Step 4</b></p></td><td align="left" class="" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">She handed <b>all</b> of the monies she withdrew to TSM.</p></td></tr></tbody></table>

  
  

153    Before this, Quah had agreed that _all_ of the payments she collected from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex belonged to the Company. She also agreed that she should therefore withdraw the _full_ sum corresponding to the cash cheques from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex. This was because she was not allowed to keep _any_ of the monies from _any_ cash cheque for her personal use. I set out her evidence on these aspects:[^248]

> Q:    Now, I want you to focus again on \[your Compilation\]…Now you explained that \[your Compilation\] allegedly only relates to cash cheques from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex, agree?

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **Now, you agree that any cash cheques that these 3 companies give to you for diesel payment actually belong\[s\] to \[the Company\], agree?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> Q:    And you have explained according to you that you banked these cash cheques into your personal bank accounts and you withdraw the money and you handed to \[TSM\], agree?

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **And because these cash cheques actually belong to \[the Company\]?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **When you withdraw the in-cash sum, you should withdraw the entire amount corresponding to the cheque, correct?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **Because you are not allowed to keep any of the money relating to those cash cheques for your personal use, agree?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

154    The withdrawals that Quah listed in her Compilation _only_ comprised four round figures: $500; $800, $1,000; and $15,000.[^249] But, _none_ of the cash cheques she collected from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex were for these round figures. In fact, the _majority_ of the cash cheques she collected were _not_ for round figures.[^250] Quah’s Compilation therefore contradicted her stance on the cash cheques she collected from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex[^251] (because the withdrawals she listed in her Compilation _did not match_ the sums stated on the cash cheques she deposited into her three personal accounts). Indeed, the Compilation showed that Quah _never_ withdrew the full sum corresponding to _any_ of the cash cheques she deposited into her personal accounts.

155    To explain away this glaring discrepancy (which showed, at the very least, that she kept part of the monies from the cash cheques she deposited into her personal accounts), Quah made numerous fresh claims when confronted during cross-examination. The belated nature of her claims (all of which were material to her defence, and which should have been raised during the cross-examination of the Prosecution’s witnesses and in her own evidence-in-chief) undermined her credibility severely. It also concurrently undercut her defence that she had been acting under TSM’s instructions at all times. I now set out _some_ of these claims – which were clearly afterthoughts.

##### Quah’s many fresh claims to account for a single cash cheque

156    In cross-examination, the Prosecution showed Quah the image of a cash cheque she had collected from Win Tran (**P7**). Dated 31 October 2011, this cheque was for the sum of $12,000.[^252] Behind this cheque, Quah had written to instruct the bank to deposit a total of $7,500 into the following accounts:

<table align="left" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="Judg-2-tblr" frame="all" pgwide="1"><colgroup><col width="11.64%"> <col width="88.36%"> </colgroup><tbody><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Sum</b></p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Destination</b></p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$6,000</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">Quah’s Cashline Account</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$1,000</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">Quah’s mother’s account</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$500</p></td><td align="left" class="" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">Unidentified account, which did not belong to Quah, TSM, or the Company</p></td></tr></tbody></table>

  
  

157    This cheque was encashed on 31 October 2011 itself. Quah agreed that $6,000 was indeed deposited into her Cashline Account on 31 October 2011 (a fact confirmed in the corresponding bank statement).[^253] She also agreed that her defence was that she had withdrawn the _full_ sum of $6,000 from her Cashline Account and handed it to TSM “very shortly after” it was deposited:[^254]

> Q:    So, if I understand your defence correctly, after this $6,000 was deposited you must have withdrawn the entire sum surely after and hand\[ed\] it \[to TSM\], correct?

> A:    I handed over 6,000 to him in cash.

> …

> Q:    …Your own account, let’s go step-by-step. Step 1, you collect cash cheques from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex.

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    Step 2, you deposit these cash cheques into your personal bank account\[s\].

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    The same bank accounts that are reflected in \[your Compilation\].

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    Step 3, you withdraw **all** the money relating to these cash cheques from these bank accounts.

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    Step 4, you hand over **all** of this money to \[TSM\].

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **So, we are clear on that? We agreed on that?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> Q:    So, now I want you to focus your mind on P7. So, this is a cheque from Win Tran agree?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    So, it is one of those that you would have deposited into one of \[your\] personal bank accounts?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    And specifically, actually we’re right now looking at the partial deposit into your \[Cashline Account\] for $6,000. And I want you to remember that we have just been through step 1, step 2, step 3, step 4 of how you would encash a cheque and withdraw the amount and hand it to \[TSM\].

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **So, if you have deposited $6,000 from a Win Tran cheque into your \[Cashline Account\], you should very shortly after withdraw $6,000. So that you can hand it to \[TSM\], agree?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **And this is according to your own defence.**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

158    Quah’s attention was then drawn to her Compilation. The Compilation showed that she had made three withdrawals totalling _only_ $2,500 (as opposed to $6,000) from her Cashline Account in November 2011. This sum was $3,500 less than the $6,000 she had deposited into her account on 31 October 2011. When confronted with this discrepancy, Quah admitted that she kept some of the monies from the cash cheques she deposited into her personal accounts (and therefore did not withdraw the full sums corresponding to these cheques). In addition, she agreed she _knew_ that it was wrong for her to keep the monies from the cash cheques she deposited in her accounts.[^255]

159    That said, she claimed that she had an arrangement with TSM where she would give him her _own_ cash first whenever he wanted money. She would then deduct the sum she had already given to TSM from the monies she later encashed from the cash cheques she deposited into her own accounts. According to Quah, she had “no choice” but to give TSM her own money first as he was “chasing after \[her\] for money all the time” and she did not have the time to go to the bank every day to withdraw cash for him:[^256]

> Q:    This \[sum of $2,500 shown in the Compilation\] is much less…it’s in fact…slightly more than one-third \[of\] the $6,000 you received in \[your Cashline Account\] on 31st October 2011.

> A:    Yes, but I want to add…I gave him $2,500 because every day he will be chasing after me for money. I gave him my own money because you ask me why I only withdraw $2,500, right?

> Q:    Actually, all I ask\[ed\] you to do was to agree or disagree that 2,500 is much less than the 6,000 you received in your account on 31st October 2011.

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**     But, since you mentioned, Madam Quah, you said I gave him my own money, **are you now suggesting that in fact you did not withdraw the full sums from the cash cheques that you deposited into your personal bank accounts?**

> **A:**      **I did not.**

> Q:    So, you are admitting that you kept some of the money from the cash cheques that you deposited into your personal bank accounts?

> A:    I also have some cash in my own personal bank account, and…our transaction is in such a way that whenever he wanted money, I would give him my personal money, cash money to him first. And then I would deduct from the cash cheque that I deposited for his share.

> **Q:**      **Now, actually, my question was, so you kept some of the monies from the cash cheques \[you\] deposited into your bank accounts and I want you to give me a “yes or no” answer.**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> Q:    And when I ask\[ed\] you earlier in cross-examination \[if\] you agree that you cannot keep amounts from cash cheques because they belong to the company, you agree\[d\]?

> A:    Yes, I agree.

> **Q:**      **So, actually you knew that it was wrong to keep money from the cash cheque\[s\] in your personal bank accounts but you did it anyway?**

> **A:**      **I agree** but at that time I ha\[d\] no choice because he was chasing after me for money all the time. Where would I have the time to go to the bank everyday to withdraw money for him?

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

160    In this case, Quah claimed she only withdrew $2,500 from her account to give to TSM as she had already given $3,500 of her own cash to him.[^257] No documentary evidence was adduced to show that she did actually give either of these sums to TSM.

161    The Prosecution then pointed out that Quah’s version was _different_ from her initial stance on the cash cheques she collected from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex (_viz_., that she withdrew **all** of the monies corresponding to these cheques from her three personal accounts and gave **all** of the monies withdrawn to TSM). Quah agreed, and proceeded to furnish an even more detailed version involving TSM _and_ Ho – which she never raised during their cross-examinations and in her own examination-in-chief:[^258]

> **Q:**      **Now, Madam Quah, you are now saying that you kept some money from the cash cheques that you deposited into your personal bank accounts, you agree?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **And you agree that this is different from what we just went through 10 minutes ago when we went through step 1, step 2, step 3, step 4 of your defence.**

> **A:**      **Yes, I agree that it’s different**, but every day…I would have to give \[TSM\] money. Every time he sees a cheque, he will want money. I know that I’m in the wrong for depositing into my bank account and only giving him a portion. But because I have already given him cash upfront previously, I would definitely have to deduct it, right? I know that it is wrong to do that, because just now, \[Prosecution\], you said 1, 2, 3, 4. I agree with you. I agree with what you said. I do not know how to put it. Every time I receive a cash cheque, I would have to sit down with \[TSM\] to talk about how he was going to get the money. Because…Eric Ho’s cheques will be blank. He did not fill in anything at all. So I cannot just retrieve cash for \[TSM\]. I would need to speak to Eric Ho and when Eric Ho gave me the instructions, then I can bank in the cheque.

> Q:    Okay, Madam Quah, I have given you quite a lot of latitude to explain yourself and when I said step 1, 2, 3, 4, I think it’s also important not to misrepresent what I asked you and you remember that when I asked you step 1, 2, 3, 4, I asked you to confirm that this is your defence.

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    And out of complete fairness to you, Madam Quah, do you want me to go through step 1, 2, 3, 4 again?

> A:    There’s no need to.

> Q:    Alright. So what we can agree on, looking at \[your Compilation\], looking at…those withdrawals for November 2011, the sum of $2,500 doesn’t match the sum of $6,000.

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **You didn’t withdraw that whole sum \[of\] $6,000 from \[your Cashline Account\] and hand it to \[TSM\].**

> …

> **A:**      **Yes, but I have already given the cash portion to him.**

> **Q:**      **And so you have given us now a new explanation that you separately gave cash to \[TSM\].**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **And now under cross-examination, it is the first time that you are raising this allegation, do you agree?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

162    As for the shortfall of $6,000 (since the cash cheque that Quah collected from Win Tran was for the total sum of $12,000), Quah claimed that TSM had:[^259]

> (a)     told her that he wanted only $6,000 in cash; and

> (b)     wanted her to deposit the remaining $6,000 into her own account because “he did not want to get so much money on that day”.

163    I noted that the remaining sum from this cash cheque appeared to be $4,500 instead of $6,000. This was because Quah had also instructed the bank to deposit a total of $1,500 into her mother’s account and another unidentified account that did not belong to her, TSM, or the Company.[^260] However, neither party pursued this during the trial. That said, on Quah’s evidence alone, it showed that she failed to deposit the remaining $6,000 into her own accounts (as she claimed TSM had instructed her to). Instead, she deposited $1,000 into her mother’s account and $500 into an unidentified account.

164    In any event, Quah’s account as to the remaining $6,000[^261] was _another_ claim she failed to raise during TSM’s cross-examination and in her examination-in-chief. It also appeared inconsistent with her fresh claim that she had “no choice” but to give TSM her own cash first as he was supposedly “chasing after \[her\] for money all the time”.[^262] If TSM was indeed doing so, why would he allow Quah to deposit a substantial sum of $6,000 into her own account, with no instructions for this large sum to be given to him at some later date? Quah never explained this inconsistency.

165    I did not give _any_ weight to all of Quah’s fresh claims. That said, I bore in mind her _admission_ that she did keep part of the monies from the cash cheques she encashed by depositing into her personal accounts.

166    The shifting nature of Quah’s evidence was most evident on the second day of her cross-examination. On that day, she gave two further accounts about the circumstances surrounding this _same_ cash cheque. She _first_ claimed that she deposited $6,000 from this cheque into her account because TSM owed her $6,000.[^263] Given Quah’s claim that TSM owed _her_ $6,000, the Prosecution pointed out that it was illogical for Quah to have then withdrawn $2,500 from her account to give to TSM (since she did not owe TSM any money). Quah disagreed, and _then_ claimed that TSM had also wanted her to give him an _additional_ sum of $3,000 to $5,000 on the first week of every month[^264] to pay the monthly instalments in respect of his car loan. Quah never raised either of these claims during TSM’s cross-examination and in her examination-in-chief. I therefore did not give them _any_ weight.

167    Quah’s various versions were all clearly afterthoughts – to explain the discrepancies between the sums stated on the cash cheques (which she admitted to encashing by depositing into her three personal accounts) and the very different sums she actually withdrew from her accounts soon after (as listed in her Compilation). Indeed, she admitted as much during her cross-examination:[^265]

> Q:    Now, Mdm Quah, we spent…a lot of time on this point, so I’m really just going to summarise. Now, number 1, yesterday, you said that you kept $3,500 being the difference…between \[$\]6,000 and $2,500 and you knew it was wrong. This was version 1, this was what you said yesterday, agree?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    Today, you g\[a\]ve version 2. You said \[TSM\] owed you $6,000. So, it’s okay to keep $6,000, you agree?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    And then after that, I pointed out to you that it’s illogical to give money back to \[TSM\] because you said he owed it to you…Because I put it to you that it is illogical because \[TSM\] was returning money to you, why would you need to withdraw $2,500?

> A:    I disagree.

> Q:    And that’s when you gave version 3, a completely new explanation about payment for \[TSM’s\] car.

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **And, really, the reason why we have had three versions in relation to the same cheque is because you know the numbers don’t add up, Mdm Quah.**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

##### More fresh claims to account for another cash cheque

168    In cross-examination, Quah was shown the image of another cash cheque she had collected from Win Tran (**P8**). Dated 29 June 2012, this cheque was for the sum of $12,000.[^266] Behind this cheque, Quah had written to instruct the bank to deposit the full sum of $12,000 into her Cashline Account. This cheque was encashed on 2 July 2012.

169    Quah’s attention was then drawn to her Compilation, which showed that she had made five withdrawals totalling _only_ $3,000 (as opposed to $12,000) from her Cashline Account in July 2012. Quah then testified that she had instead encashed the full sum of $12,000 _over the counter_ on 2 July 2012. She said she then passed this full sum to TSM later that same day.[^267] According to Quah, she never deposited this cheque into her Cashline Account. As such, it was not reflected in her Compilation.

170    Quah’s account in relation to this cheque was, however, _again_ different from her initial stance on the cash cheques she collected from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex (_viz_, she deposited **all** of the cash cheques she collected into her three personal bank accounts, as reflected in her Compilation). It was never raised during TSM’s cross-examination and in her examination-in-chief. Indeed, it was only during her cross-examination that Quah testified that she did not bank all of the cash cheques she collected from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex into her three personal accounts. I therefore did not give _any_ weight to this fresh claim. In any event, Quah conceded that she had no evidence showing that she _did_ actually hand any cash from this cheque to TSM.[^268]

##### Further fresh claims in relation to sums shown in Compilation

171    It was Quah’s position that her Compilation showed that she withdrew a total of $451,400 from her three personal bank accounts between 2 January 2010 and 16 August 2014. According to Quah, this sum came from the cash cheques issued by EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex (that she deposited into her accounts).[^269] Cross-examination revealed _more_ flaws in her evidence.

Quah had no receipts showing she handed $451,400 to TSM

172    In cross-examination, Quah _agreed_ that her Compilation did not show that she did actually hand $451,400 in cash to TSM (or indeed _any_ sum of cash to TSM).[^270] She _initially_ testified that she did not have any documentary evidence showing that she handed this sum of $451,400 to TSM.[^271] But, she changed her evidence immediately after when the Prosecution questioned why she did not notwithstanding her long experience “working with accounts”. She then claimed (for the _first_ time) that she had receipts showing that she handed $451,400 in cash to TSM in her computer in the Office. She also claimed that TSM had signed these receipts to acknowledge receipt of the cash she had allegedly handed him. She testified that she had “no time to copy \[these receipts\] out” as she “needed to buy a thumb drive to copy the information out”. She said she was not able to do this in time as TSM had asked her to leave the Office immediately after she entered it on 25 August 2014 and she could not enter the Office again thereafter.[^272]

173    Quah agreed that this was the first time she was raising the existence of these receipts. When pressed by the Prosecution, her inconsistent and evasive responses showed that this claim about the existence of receipts – which would have been _highly_ material to her case – was clearly an afterthought. Her exchange with the Prosecution bear setting out in full to appreciate the unsatisfactory nature of her belated evidence:

> Q:    And you agree, Mdm Quah, that actually, this entire thing about receipts showing that \[TSM\] received the cash from you today…is the first time you are raising it?

> **A:**      **Because you asked me about it, so I answered.**

> **Q:**      **\[TSM\] was not asked about this, when \[he\] came to Court. You agree?**

> **A:**      **Nobody asked me.**

> ...

> **Q:**      **Now, Mdm Quah, I just want to be very clear that I understood your answer correctly. Because, actually, \[my\] question to you was very simple. It was that from April 2015 \[when investigations commenced\] to 16th January 2019, today was the first day you mentioned the issue of receipts. Yes or no?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> Q:    So, actually, you concede that you did not raise it to the IO?

> A:    I did challenge him.

> Q:    Mdm Quah, the question is very simple. Did you or did you not raise \[the issue of receipts\] to the IO?

> A:    I did.

> **Q:**      **So, now it’s different. I give you one more chance to clarify. First question was, between April 2015 and 16th January 2019, today is the first day you mentioned the receipts. Do you agree or disagree?**

> **A:**      **Yes, I agree.**

> Q:    Logically, that means you could not have mentioned it to the IO?

> A:    This is the first time I’m raising this in Court that I had the evidence…But I did mention this to the IO before. I told him to go to the office to get the system out. In my computer systems, there were a lot of information. Did he get them? No, he also did not have any records.

> **Q:**      **I want you to focus your mind on the receipts, Mdm Quah that’s the only thing we are talking about now. We are keeping it very simple. So, you answer my questions clearly, okay? Did you or did you not mention the issue of receipts for $451,400 to the IO? Yes or no?**

> **A:**      **No, because at that time, there were no bank statements to refer to.**

> **Q:**      **Yah, but the receipts have nothing to do with the bank statements. You agree? Because if you…had the receipts, the receipts would show that you gave cash to \[TSM\]. Agree? Agree or disagree?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> Q:    And you also accepted that apart from the 2 issues you pointed out, all your statements \[recorded by\] the police \[were\] accurately recorded.

> A:    Yes, at that time I did say that.

> Q:    With the exception, in all fairness to you, of \[the\] 2 \[unrelated\] points that we went through yesterday.

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    So, the truth of the matter is that you did not mention the receipts of $451,400 to the police?

> A:    I did say. But it was not written down.

> **Q:**      **You’re being inconsistent, Mdm Quah.**

> **A:**      **No.**

> **Q:**      **In any event it’s very clear that these receipts issue, today is the first day that you have mentioned it in Court.**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> Q:    Even though you agree it’s quite important.

> A:    I did tell the IO…and asked him to go to my office…\[t\]o take the computer system to go to Cantonment CID to ask the CAD officer to open up and see what is…in my computer \[as spoken by Witness in English\].

> Q:    Mdm Quah, I’m going to have to remind you to speak in Mandarin.

> A:    But it was not written. Now, you are telling me that I did not say. At that time, I have already told him about it but he ignored me.

> **Q:**      **Okay. Mdm Quah, so we’ve been through quite a few iterations on this whether you told the IO, you didn’t tell the IO, whether you said in Court, didn’t say in Court…**

> **A:**      **Then let’s not waste time.**

> Q:    So, really, I’m just going to put it to you that my case is that, you \[are\] not being truthful. There are no receipts.

> A:    I disagree. There were receipts on the computer.

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

174    I did not give _any_ weight to Quah’s fresh claim as to the existence of these receipts. Their existence would have been _very_ significant to her case. Yet, she never raised this critical fact during the cross-examinations of the IO and TSM. She also did not raise it during her examination-in-chief. I agreed with the Prosecution that this claim was “clearly a concocted…afterthought as \[Quah\] realised the weight of the evidence against her”.[^273] These receipts _never_ existed.

To account for excess not reflected in Compilation

175    In cross-examination, Quah _agreed_ that she had collected _more than_ $451,400 from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex.[^274] In fact, she _agreed_ that she had collected a total of $575,788.89 in cash cheques from these customers. This larger sum was stated in a table the Prosecution had prepared (**P26**). It was based on a breakdown of the sums stated in the charges, which appeared in the IO’s conditioned statement. The table prepared by the Prosecution (“the Summation”) listed all the cash cheques issued by EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex.[^275] The figure of $575,788.89 in the Summation was $124,388.89 more than the sum of $451,400 that Quah had reflected in her Compilation.

176    In cross-examination, Quah _agreed_ that her Compilation did not explain this excess of $124,388.89. However, she claimed – again, for the _first_ time – that this was because she had encashed cash cheques corresponding to this excess over the counter at the bank. According to Quah, she had then immediately handed the cash to TSM.[^276]

177    Yet, Quah also agreed that she had deposited _all_ of the cash cheques she collected from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex into her three personal accounts.[^277] This effectively contradicted her evidence that she had encashed cash cheques amounting to $124,388.89 over the counter at the bank. In addition, I noted that she did not refer to the existence of any “receipts” where TSM acknowledged receipt of $124,388.89 in cash from her.[^278]

178    There appeared to be a further inconsistency in Quah’s evidence. She had earlier claimed that she did not have the time to go to the bank every day to withdraw cash for TSM.[^279] Yet, she now claimed that she was able to encash $124,388.89 worth of cash cheques over the counter _at the bank_. Quah never explained this inconsistency. She also did not explain why the majority of the cash cheques were deposited into her accounts, and why some had to be encashed over the counter instead. In fact, Quah _agreed_ that this latter version differed from her initial stance on the cash cheques she collected from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex (_viz_., she deposited **all** the cash cheques she collected into her three personal bank accounts). She initially appeared to disagree that it was a convenient afterthought to explain away the discrepancy between her Compilation and the Summation.[^280] But, she later accepted that she had changed her evidence because she could not reconcile her initial stance with the Summation. I set out Quah’s evidence:[^281]

> Q:    \[After confirming with Quah her initial version in relation to the cash cheques she collected from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex.\] So you agree that was version 1, step 1, step 2, step 3, step 4?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    But you have now given version 2, you agree?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    Because now you are telling me that, “No, no, no, not all of the cash cheques went into my personal bank accounts.”

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    You are now telling me that you encashed some of them over the counter?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    And then you immediately handed it over?

> A:    To \[TSM\].

> **Q:**      **And this is a new version, you agree it’s different from version 1?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **And really, Mdm Quah, I’m going to suggest \[to\] you that the reason you have changed your evidence is because when I gave you \[the Summation\], you realised that the number doesn’t match \[your Compilation\].**

> **A:**      **I agree.**

> Q:    So, you agree you changed your evidence when I confronted you with the accurate documentary evidence?

> A:    I agree, because the figures – because there was a difference of…124,000. I would definitely want to defend myself.

> Q:    So, really, the fact that this explanation is only coming now, Mdm Quah, shows that it is a convenient afterthought?

> A:    No…it was only this morning that you gave me \[the Summation\] when I look at my documents, whatever you gave to my lawyer and my lawyer showed to me.

> …

> Q:    Mdm Quah, I…asked you to agree whether it was a convenient afterthought, the fact that you have now given version 2 of your evidence. And you disagreed with me. You then started to go into an explanation relating to your conversations with your lawyer. So, it’s up to you whether you want to continue with that answer.

> A:    I feel that I should not continue.

> **Q:**      **Certainly. Now, Mdm Quah, I’m just going to point out to you that the \[Summation\] of cash cheques is really a subset of the amounts in the charges against you?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **And all the relevant documents that were meant to be disclosed to you were, in fact, disclosed to you.**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> …

> Q:    Now, Mdm Quah, I just want to make it very clear to you that you were the one who raised version 1 of your defence with steps 1 to 4, you agree?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    It’s your own explanation of what happened to the cash cheques from Time-Flex, EDS and Win Tran?

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **So, when I gave you \[the Summation\], I was merely pointing out to you what you should already know?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **And so, really, you’ve changed your evidence now, because there is no way to reconcile version 1 with the \[Summation\] that I have given to you?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

179    I did not give _any_ weight to Quah’s fresh claim that she encashed $124,388.89 worth of cash cheques over the counter at the bank. It was inconsistent with her initial stance on these cash cheques (a stance she confirmed _several_ times during her cross-examination). Her fresh claim was clearly an afterthought to explain away the discrepancy between her Compilation and the Summation.

Alleged “Cash Withdrawal Practice” an afterthought

180    In closing submissions, Counsel made multiple references to an alleged “Cash Withdrawal Practice”. Counsel had described this practice, together with the alleged “Cash Collection Practice” and “Encashing Practice”[^282] in the following terms:

> **IV**.     **THE DEFENCE’S CASE, AND THE ISSUES ARISING**

> …

> 45.    At all material times, \[Quah\] was acting under TSM’s instructions.

> 46.    In particular, \[Quah\] was instructed by TSM to collect cash, or cash cheques as payments from the \[Clients\], and hand the cash to him in the following manner:

>> (1)    In respect of the cash collected, \[Quah\] would hand the cash collected to TSM directly (“**Cash Collection Practice**”).

>> (2)    In respect of the cash cheques collected (save for the \[cash cheques from EDS that Quah deposited into the Joint Account[^283]\]:

>>> (a)    Initially, \[Quah\] would encash the cash cheques over bank counters for cash, and hand the cash collected to TSM (“**Encashing Practice**”)

>>> (b)    Subsequently, \[Quah\] would deposit the cash cheques into various \[personal\] bank accounts \[of hers\]. All the cash from the cash cheques that were deposited directly into bank accounts other than the \[Company’s Account\] and the \[Joint Account\] was accounted for: \[Quah\] gave her “_personal money_” to TSM, and where \[Quah\] deposited the cash cheques into \[her Cashline Account, Savings Account, and CashPlus Account\], she would withdraw cash in sums of S$500, S$800, and S$1,000, and hand the cash withdrawn to TSM (collectively, “**Cash Withdrawal Practice**”).

> \[emphasis in original\]

181    These submissions completely ignored the fact that Quah only raised the “Cash Withdrawal Practice” when she was cross-examined. She never raised it during TSM’s cross-examination and in her evidence-in-chief.[^284] Indeed, it was clear from the manner in which her evidence on this practice arose that it was “nothing more than a convenient afterthought”.[^285] I set out her testimony in full in **Annex A** to these grounds, and summarise it in the paragraphs that follow.

182    In cross-examination, Quah agreed with the Prosecution that her Compilation did _not_ include _all_ the cash withdrawals she made from her three personal bank accounts. To illustrate this point, the Prosecution referred Quah to a bank statement for her Cashline Account. This showed a “CASH WITHDRAWAL AT ATM” of $1,000 on 5 September 2010.[^286] The Prosecution noted that Quah had not included this withdrawal in her Compilation (which only listed withdrawals on 2, 7, 15, 28, and 29 September 2010). In response, Quah testified that she had made a “mistake”. She claimed that the date corresponding to a $1,000 withdrawal on 2 September 2010 (as she had reflected in her Compilation) should have been 5 September 2010 instead.[^287]

183    The Prosecution then pointed out that this was actually _not_ a “mistake”. This was because Quah’s bank statement[^288] did _in fact_ show a “CASH WITHDRAWAL AT ATM” of $1,000 on 2 September 2010. On seeing this, Quah back-pedalled and _agreed_ that she had not made a mistake. I had observed Quah when she testified. It was clear that she had been caught out. She then tried to explain her omission of this withdrawal from her Compilation by claiming that it had been “very difficult” to analyse her bank statements. I found this difficult to believe. After all, these were _her_ bank statements showing _her_ withdrawals from _her_ accounts. The Prosecution had also disclosed all of _her_ bank statements at the pre-trial stage – sometime in early-June 2018. Given that she only tendered this Compilation in mid-January 2019, she had more than six months (at the very least) to review _her_ statements. The words “CASH WITHDRAWAL AT ATM” were also clearly stated on her statements.

184    Quah then agreed with the Prosecution that she had “suddenly remembered” (during her cross-examination) that this $1,000 withdrawal on 5 September 2010 should have been included in her Compilation (as such, she said that her Compilation should have indicated a total withdrawal of $451,400 _and_ $1,000, or $452,400). She went on to claim that this $1,000 withdrawal on 5 September 2010 was specifically from the cash cheques she had collected from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex (which she had deposited into her Cashline Account). She further claimed that she had “definitely” handed this sum to TSM.[^289] I did not find these claims believable as well. It was difficult to understand how Quah could so definitively testify as to the source and destination of these monies when more than eight years had passed. This was especially in light of her supposed difficulty in analysing her bank statements.[^290]

185    To bolster the point that Quah’s Compilation did not include _all_ the cash withdrawals she made from her three personal accounts, the Prosecution referred her to another bank statement for the Cashline Account.[^291] This showed two instances of “CASH WITHRAWAL AT ATM” for $200 (on 29 October 2010) and $400 (on 30 October 2010). Quah agreed that her Compilation did _not_ reflect these two withdrawals as well. She also agreed that it was _entirely possible_ that she had made these two withdrawals to pay for her personal expenses. Indeed, she accepted that she _did_ withdraw cash for her personal expenses from her three personal accounts. That said, she denied that it was possible that her $1,000 withdrawal on 5 September 2010 might have also been for her personal expenses. Immediately after, however, she:[^292]

> (a)      _agreed_ that she had no way of telling whether this withdrawal was indeed related to the cash cheques she collected from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex (hence contradicting her earlier evidence[^293]).

> (b)     effectively testified that she had “_no_ system” of telling whether her withdrawals from her three personal accounts were:

>> (i)       related to the cash cheques she collected from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex (which she had deposited into her accounts);

>> (ii)       for her personal expenses; or

>> (iii)       supposedly passed to TSM.

186    It was _only_ when the Prosecution put it to Quah that she had “no system of telling which cash withdrawals \[were\] for handing money to \[TSM\] and \[which\] were for \[her\] personal expenses” that Quah raised the “Cash Withdrawal Practice”.[^294] In this regard, she claimed that she would _always_ give the $500 and $1,000 withdrawals from her three personal accounts to TSM.[^295] According to Quah, she had used $500 and $1,000 as a “convenient mark” to indicate that she had handed these sums to TSM.[^296] She also claimed that she could identify the withdrawals meant for her personal expenses because she _always_ withdrew less than $500 for such expenses.

187    I gave _no_ weight to Quah’s evidence on this alleged “Cash Withdrawal Practice”. It was clearly an afterthought, to explain how she could differentiate the withdrawals she made from her three personal accounts. It was an attempt to concoct a “system”, when none existed.[^297] If this had indeed been Quah’s consistent practice between July 2009 and June 2014 (_ie_, a period of _five_ years), it was not at all clear why she failed to raise it:

> (a)     during TSM’s cross-examination;

> (b)     in her evidence-in-chief; and

> (c)     when the Prosecution initially referred her to the three withdrawals from her Cashline Account.[^298]

188    In fact, when the Prosecution asked Quah to explain why she failed to raise the “Cash Withdrawal Practice” earlier, she could only reply, “I do not know”.[^299]

189    The Prosecution also highlighted another inconsistency in Quah’s evidence on the “Cash Withdrawal Practice” – it was at odds with her earlier evidence that TSM had instructed her to withdraw the _full_ sum corresponding to the cash cheques she deposited into her personal accounts. The Prosecution also observed that Counsel’s closing submissions had sought to advance an explanation for some of the cash cheques from EDS that Quah never testified on. I set out the Prosecution’s submissions on these aspects, which I agreed with:[^300]

> 31    Crucially, there is an important difference between (a) encashing cash cheques and passing \[the full amount of the corresponding\] cash to TSM; and (b) depositing the cash cheques into personal bank accounts, and deciding at one’s absolute discretion to give TSM sums \[of cash in denominations\] of $500, $800 and $1,000 \[that do not amount to the full sum of cash enchased from the cash cheques\].

> 32    In fact, \[Quah’s\] own evidence was that \[TSM told her\] to cash out \[all of\] the money and go back to the office immediately to hand the money to TSM:[^301]

>> Q:    Okay. Madam Quah, let \[me\] just pause you there. Earlier in your evidence, you said that you had shared with \[TSM\] that it was very tough and wasting your time for you to queue at the bank, to cash out the cash cheques. And then you gave evidence that \[TSM\] told you that you could deposit the cash cheque into your account instead. So did \[TSM\] then also tell you what you are supposed to do with the cash that \[had\] been deposited into your account?

>> **A:**      **To cash out the money and \[go\] back to the office immediately to hand it to him.**

>> Q:    And were you able to do so?

>> **A:**      **Yes. Because \[LEC\] would drive me to the bank.**

>> Q:    So you are saying for cash cheques that were deposited into your accounts, you could immediately withdraw the cash out?

>> A:    If \[TSM\] wanted the money I would give him my personal money first. Because for cash cheque, even \[if\] you deposited it \[today\] you would have to wait until 2:00pm on the next day to withdraw.

> 33    \[Quah’s\] own explanation is therefore inherently untenable. On one hand, she claims that she had TSM’s consent and instructions to deposit the cash cheques into her account\[s\] and withdraw the \[full\] sum \[corresponding to the cash cheques\]. On the other hand, she appears (based on her own account \[in cross-examination\] to have unilaterally decided that it was up to her to decide how to deal with the monies, and that she could simply withdraw them from her own account in the denominations that she pleased. Conveniently, she only raised much of this under cross-examination; tellingly, despite being the accounts manager, she has raised no objective evidence in her favour.

> 34    Moreover, the Defence raises at \[61\] of its closing submissions _yet another different explanation_ for some of the EDS cash cheques, as compared with \[Quah’s\] evidence under cross-examination. They cite extracts from \[Quah’s 7th investigation statement: AB26\] in which she state\[d\]:

>> (a)    She had encashed DBS/E003 cheque for S$16,329.48 and handed over the entire sum in cash denominations of $50 and $10 notes.

>> (b)    She had either banked in DBS302512 cheque for S$12,000 and cashed it out, or encashed it, and handed over the entire sum in denominations of S$50 to \[TSM\].

> 35    \[Quah\] has therefore shown herself to give different explanations based on what suits her defence – it has changed from encashing certain specific \[cash\] cheques and handing over the sum in full; to withdrawing sums of S$500, S$800 and S$1000 after depositing the cheques into her personal bank accounts. It is clear that she herself cannot account for each sum.

> \[emphasis in original\]

190    In fact, Quah went on to claim (again, for the _first_ time) that it was TSM who had directed her to only withdraw cash in denominations of $500 or $1,000 from her personal accounts. She later said that this practice was also pursuant to an agreement she had with TSM, so that it would be “easier for \[TSM\] to check and also for \[her\] to check” as TSM checked her bank statements “every month, every year”.[^302] I gave no weight to these claims as well. Quah’s evidence effectively meant that TSM permitted her to:

> (a)     choose the sums she withdrew from her personal accounts, no matter the amounts stated on the cash cheques from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex; and

> (b)     mix the remaining amounts from these cash cheques (which belonged to the Company), no matter how much, with her personal funds in her accounts and apply them to her personal expenses.

191    But, she failed to raise any of these aspects during TSM’s cross-examination. Indeed, when asked to explain her failure to do so, she could only say that nobody had asked her.[^303] This was hardly an explanation.

##### Quah deposited numerous cash cheques into credit lines

192    The special nature of Quah’s Cashline and CashPlus accounts – two of the three personal bank accounts into which Quah had deposited the cash cheques she collected – was raised during her cross-examination. These accounts were neither current accounts nor savings accounts. They were instead lines of credit that allowed Quah to access cash whenever she needed it. She thus had to pay interest to the respective banks whenever she withdrew cash from these accounts. In other words, whenever Quah withdrew (or, more accurately, borrowed) money from these accounts to give to TSM (as she claimed she did), she had to pay interest to the banks.[^304] The evidence showed that Quah deposited _numerous_ cash cheques into these two accounts, even though she also had a savings account (into which she had only deposited some of the cash cheques she collected).

Quah’s Cashline Account (a credit line)

193    It made no sense for Quah to borrow money from the bank to give to TSM, and incur interest at the same time. After all, the money that she was borrowing from the bank (to supposedly pass to TSM) was not even going to be hers to keep. She could have simply just encashed these cash cheques and not incur _any_ interest. The absurdity of Quah’s evidence was evident when she was questioned by the Prosecution:[^305]

> Q:    So, in effect, when you withdraw money from this account, you have to pay interest on it?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    In other words, you are, in fact, borrowing money from the bank.

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    And you claimed that you were depositing cash cheques into this bank account for the purpose of withdrawing the cash to hand to \[TSM\]. Mdm Quah can you just agree or disagree?

> A:    Yes.

> …

> **Q:**      **In other words, you were borrowing money from the bank with interest to pay \[TSM\]?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **And you agree that this is quite illogical?**

> **A:**      **I agree.**

> **Q:**      **Because if, really, all you needed to do was \[to\] encash the cash cheque, you shouldn’t be having to pay interest?**

> **A:**      **It is very troublesome to have to queue at the counter…**

> Q:    Okay. Mdm Quah, this is, in fact, a false dichotomy. So, you have claimed that you deposited cash cheques into a Cashline account instead of going to the bank to encash it over the counter.

> A:    Yes, it’s troublesome.

> **Q:**      **So, you would rather pay interest on company money than go to the bank?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **Even though going to the bank is part of your responsibilities as an employee?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **And, of course, this doesn’t even answer the question of why you didn’t deposit the cash cheques into a normal savings account?**

> A:    All along, I have only been using Cashine \[from\] DBS.

> **Q:**      **So, really, what this suggest\[s\] is that you were using the company’s cheques to pay off your own debts.**

> **A:**      **No.**

> **Q:**      **Because if they really were for payments to \[TSM\], you wouldn’t withdraw from an account that you have to pay interest on?**

> **A:**      **I’m already very used to doing it.**

> **Q:**      **So, are you suggesting that out of your own generosity, you \[were\] content to pay interest \[in respect of\] cash cheques belonging to the company?**

> **A:**      **It’s just tens of dollars, it means nothing to me.**

> **Q:**      **But, actually, you agreed with me earlier that you also used this bank account for personal expenses. Yes?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

194    Quah’s attention was also drawn to her August-September 2010 bank statement for her Cashline Account. This showed that two GIRO deductions were made from this account in September 2010 (amounting to about $102):[^306]

> (a)     by “PRUDENTIAL ASSURANCE” on 1 September 2010; and

> (b)     by “AMERICAN INTL ASS CO” on 3 September 2010.

195    These deductions both occurred _after_ Quah had deposited a cash cheque of $6,000 from Win Tran[^307] (dated 31 August 2010, and encashed on 1 September 2010) into her Cashline Account. In cross-examination, Quah _agreed_ that both of these GIRO deductions had been for her personal expenses. She also initially _agreed_ that she was “applying cash cheques deposited into \[her Cashline Account\] to other personal expenses”. However, she denied doing so when the Prosecution repeated its question:[^308]

> Q:    And so, this cheque of $6,000, I just want you to confirm that it corresponds with the payment by cheque of $6,000 into your \[Cashline Account\] on 1st September 2010.

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    And if you turn the page on AB2 to page 206. And I want you to focus on the transaction activity that occurs on 1st September 2010. The 1st line item says, “GIRO \[deduction\] by Prudential Assurance”. Do you see that, Mdm Quah?

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **You agree this was a personal expense?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> Q:    And similarly, if you just move your finger down the page on 3rd September 2010, there was also a GIRO \[deduction\] by American International Assurance Company. Do you see that?

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **\[And this was\] also a personal expense, agree?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **And these personal expenses occurred after that cheque \[of $6,000\] was deposited into this account?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **So, really, you \[were\] applying cash cheques \[that you\] deposited into \[your Cashline Account\] to other personal expenses?**

> **A:**      **Yes**. But there is interest charged to Cashline. I would rather pay interest is because I have personal items inside.

> …

> **Q:**      **So, really, you are agreeing that you used cash cheques to make payments for your personal expenses?**

> **A:**      **No, I cannot use them.**

> **Q:**      **Well, you agreed with me, Mdm Quah, that those GIRO deductions took place after the cash cheque \[of $6,000\] was deposited. So really, there’s no other way to construe it?**

> **A:**      **No, it’s not like this.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]
