272    In these circumstances, I placed no weight on the fact that TSM had not lodged the Company’s Police Report personally. Quah had appeared to suggest that TSM was somehow less credible because “\[e\]ven though TSM was obviously the appropriate party to lodge \[the report\] in respect of the unaccounted for amount, he instructed LEC to \[do so\]”.[^435] In examination-in-chief, TSM explained that he did not lodge the report personally as he was busy. He also testified that he thought that the police would investigate the matter fairly, regardless of who reported it to the police.[^436] In addition, he gave unchallenged evidence that he did not:

> (a)     give LEC any documents to pass to the police;

> (b)     give Fion any instructions about lodging the report; and

> (c)     discuss with LEC what should be reported.[^437]

273    In cross-examination, TSM testified that he was busy as he had a golf tournament. However, he disagreed that he did not lodge the Company’s Police Report personally because he “didn’t really care much about the matter against \[Quah\]”. He explained that he trusted the police to be fair in its investigations. It therefore did not matter who lodged the report. I set out TSM’s evidence:[^438]

> Q:    Okay, Mr Tan, I’m going to put it to you. The fact that you were going for a social activity instead of lodging the police report yourself shows that you didn’t really care much about this matter against Mdm Quah Siew Hong.

> A:    I disagree.

> Q:    Now, if you were really concerned, you should have made the report to the police yourself, right?

> **A:**      **Because just now I have already explained, I was thinking I had trust in our police to be fair in this. So it doesn’t matter who made the report. It will be the same. \[I\]t’s not that if I go and report this myself, I will get some special treatment.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

274    I accepted TSM’s explanation, which I found credible. One would have expected TSM to lodge the report personally (instead of asking LEC to do so) if he did indeed bear a “grudge” against Quah (or, to at least give directions to LEC or Fion as to what to report). In any event, I noted the inconsistency in Quah’s case. On the one hand, she alleged that TSM had directed LEC to make the Company’s Police Report as he “bore a grudge” against her. Yet, on the other hand, she alleged that TSM did not lodge the report personally because he “didn’t really care much about the matter against \[her\]”.

#### Immaterial that Company had yet to bring civil action against Quah

275    In cross-examination, TSM’s attention was drawn to the Demand Letters. The third letter stated that the Company’s lawyers had been instructed to commence legal action against Quah[^439], and TSM testified that he had fully authorised them to do so.[^440] It was not disputed that the Company had not commenced such action by the time the trial commenced, notwithstanding this third letter. It was then put to TSM that the Company “eventually…did not sue \[Quah\] because \[it\] did not have a genuine claim against her”. TSM forcefully disagreed with this suggestion, and explained why the Company had refrained from filing a civil claim against Quah. I set out TSM’s evidence:[^441]

> Q:    Now, Mr Tan, \[the third letter\] makes it very clear that…the \[Company’s\] lawyers will go in to sue Madam Quah.

> A:    Initially, I was told that \[the Company’s lawyers were\] going to sue her but later \[they\] told me that they are handing this over to the police.

> Q:    Now, Mr Tan. I’m going to put it to you that eventually the \[Company\] did not sue Madam Quah…because the \[Company\] did not have a genuine claim against her.

> A:    I disagree. I disagree.

> Q:    If the \[Company\] was sure that this money had been taken by Madam Quah, you would have caused the \[Company\] to sue her.

> A:    I disagree.

> Q:    The fact that a lawsuit was not started against her shows that actually, there is no claim against her for the sum of $974,020.94.

> A:    I disagree \[that\] I’m not suing her. I want to sue her.

> **Q:**      **Now, you say you want to sue her but it’s been more than 3½ years.**

> **A:**      **Because the lawyer explained to me that if we sue her, it will be very difficult for us to get a lot of the evidence. So if the government is going to investigate this, it will be much faster than \[if\] we did.**

> **Q:**      **Earlier you said it will be difficult to get evidence. What do you mean by that?**

> **A:**      **Because the lawyer said that we would have to get our clients’ bank whatever. So it’s very difficult to get.**

> Q:    Mr Tan, so basically, are you saying that you were not sure whether the \[Company\] even had a claim against Madam Quah?

> A:    That’s why I handed this over to the police. \[The police\] will be fair. And then after that, I can sue her.

> Q:    So, Mr Tan, at the end of the day you are saying that when the police report was lodged, you didn’t have any evidence against her.

> A:    When I reported this to the police, there was only this figure that was being checked by \[the Audit Firm\]. Initially, I didn’t know that the accounts would be done so ridiculously. **So when \[the Audit Firm\] checked this out and \[it\] told me to go to the police and my lawyers also told me to report this to the police.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

276    TSM’s evidence was consistent with the evidence that the Company’s Police Report was only filed at the suggestion of the Audit Firm and the Company’s lawyers.[^442] That said, the fact that the Company had yet to file a civil claim against Quah was, at best, a neutral point. The present case involved the determination of Quah’s criminal liability on the evidence presented before this court. It was irrelevant that a civil action, an entirely separate proceeding calling for the proof of different elements, had yet to be taken by the Company. Even if the Company had filed a civil claim against Quah, that was irrelevant to Quah’s criminal liability. In any event, I found that TSM’s explanation as to why the Company had yet to pursue a civil claim against Quah was reasonable.

#### TSM’s supposed status as a “shrewd businessman”

277    In cross-examination, TSM was referred to a “People Profile” search (**D12**) that listed his current appointments. This indicated that he was:

> (a)     a director of a third company, that operated from the same premises as the Company and SINCL;

> (b)     an ordinary shareholder of two other companies (apart from the Company and SINCL); and

> (c)     the owner of “SIN CHIN LIN TRADING”.

278    It was TSM’s unchallenged evidence that “SIN CHIN LIN TRADING” was “registered but there was not much business being done”.[^443] .

279    The “People Profile” search also listed TSM’s past appointments. It indicated that he had been a director of 11 other companies as well as owner and/or manager of three companies. TSM agreed that he had many business activities. However, he disagreed that he was therefore a shrewd businessman. He explained as follows:[^444]

> **A:**      **I have a lot of business but…only in name. I wasn’t the one who was actually doing them. If I’m a shrewd businessman, I would \[not\] have lost all the monies that I’ve earned in my life.**

> Q:    Mr Tan, I put it to you that you are a shrewd businessman.

> **A:**      **I am not shrewd otherwise I wouldn’t have money missing. I wouldn’t…have lost my money.**

> Q:    And I put it to you that because you are a shrewd businessman, you would have ensured that \[for\] customers who made payment to \[the Company\] \[t\]he money would have actually been received by the company?

> **A:**      **I can put it this way. My failure was that I trusted people too much. That was why I failed, that was why I had money missing.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

280    TSM’s explanation that he had “a lot of business but…only in name” and that he “wasn’t the one who was actually doing them”[^445] was not challenged. In these circumstances, I did not think that the “People Profile” search went any way to showing that TSM was in fact a “shrewd businessman”.

#### TSM had no reliable system to verify/monitor cash received

281    TSM testified that he had ceased having oversight of Quah’s work by the time of the offending period.[^446] In reply submissions, Counsel asserted that TSM’s testimony was “internally inconsistent with other parts of TSM’s evidence, and externally inconsistent with Fion’s testimony”.[^447] In this vein, Counsel submitted that:

> 5.    …Between 2009 and 2014, TSM supervised \[Quah’s\] work in the following manner:

>> (1)    TSM received cash from \[Quah\] from the Cash Collection Practice, Encashing Practice, and the Cash Withdrawal Practice, and recorded his receipt of the cash in his \[Cash Record Book\]…

>> (2)    \[There was a\] Payment Verification System \[based on the Monthly Invoice Spreadsheets\] in place for TSM to verify that payments were made by the \[Clients\], and monitor outstanding payments. The Payment Verification System was still in place in 2014, before \[Quah’s\] last day of work…

>> (3)    TSM admitted that he did, in fact, actively verify that payments were made by the \[Clients\], and monitor outstanding payments…

282    I did not find these submissions persuasive. I now elaborate.

##### Cash Record Book not means to verify/monitor

283    Counsel argued that “TSM’s conduct of recording his receipt of cash in \[the Cash Record Book\] show\[ed\] he had a means of verifying that payments were made…”[^448]

284    I noted that Fion recalled instances, between 2009 and 2014, where she saw Quah passing cash payments to TSM. On these occasions, she also saw TSM make a record in the Cash Record Book.[^449] In addition, it was her evidence that TSM would do the same when she passed him cash payments from SINCL’s clients.[^450] I noted that TSM candidly testified to similar effect:[^451]

> Q:    \[TSM\], \[Fion\] has given evidence that in the course of her work for \[SINCL\], she would sometimes collect cash payments from customers. Do you agree with this?

> …

> A:    Yes, sometimes she will collect cash.

> Q:    And she says when she collects cash, she will hand it over to you. Do you agree?

> A:    If it’s large amounts, I will ask her to deposit into the bank. If it’s small amounts, she will hand them over to me.

> Q:    Okay, \[Fion’s\] evidence is that once she receives cash, she hands it to you immediately?

> A:    I have already said that if it’s big amounts, I would ask her to go to the bank. If it’s small amounts, if she does not want to go, she would hand them over to me.

> Q:    And she says when she handed cash to you, you will write it in a book?

> A:    It’s a small booklet.

> Q:    And…when we asked her whether this book was kept in the 1st drawer of your office table, she said, “Yes.” Do you agree?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    And then we also asked her whether she saw \[Quah\] handing cash to you when she was present in the office. And she said that \[Quah\] did.

> A:    We are very open about this. So, why wouldn’t she see it --- if it’s like a few hundred dollars, it would be handed over to me.

> Q:    And we asked her as well whether you would have also recorded the cash that you received from \[Quah\] into the book. And she said, “Yes.” Do you agree?

> A:    I have already said that if it’s big amounts, then it will go to the bank. If it’s small amounts, it would be handed over to me.

> Q:    Okay, \[TSM\], listen carefully For cash that \[Quah\] hands over to you, Fion says that you would also record it in your small book.

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    Okay. So, you agree that you do record it in that book?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    Now, where is this book now?

> A:    I have thrown it away. Why should I keep it?

> Q:    So, when did you throw it away?

> A:    So, every month, when the accounts are settled, we would just erase and throw away.

> Q:    No, are you saying that you erased the entries away or you threw the book away?

> A:    So…every month when the accounts are settled…I would erase the entries away every month and every year, I would throw the booklet away.

> Q:    So, do you still have that booklet with you now?

> A:    No.

> Q:    So, you don’t keep any booklet at all now?

> A:    Now, I have stop\[ped\] doing this. All the money collected will be deposited into the bank.

285    That said, I bore in mind that TSM had forcefully denied the alleged Encashing Practice. He also denied that he instructed Quah to “deposit…cash cheque\[s\] into her account\[s\]…withdraw the cash and give it to \[him\]”.[^452] On this note, I reiterate my finding that the alleged “Cash Withdrawal Practice” was an afterthought on Quah’s part.[^453] In addition, the Entrusted Monies were distinct from the cash payments that TSM received from Quah.[^454] Fion’s evidence[^455] should thus be seen against this context. More fundamentally, the evidence showed that TSM’s _recording_ of the sums that Quah passed him was just that – a _recording_ exercise, so that the correct sum would be reflected on his personal cheque to the Company. It was not also an exercise “to _verify_ that payments were made by the \[Clients\], and _monitor_ outstanding payments”.[^456] This much was clear from TSM’s evidence in cross-examination:

> Q:    And so…if \[a\] customer had paid in cash, the cash would have been handed over to you?

> A:    Because I have said for small amounts, I would collect. For big amounts \[Quah\] would take them to the bank. **I do not know whether she really deposited them.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

##### Monthly Invoices Spreadsheet not reliable means to verify/monitor

286    In reply submissions, Counsel contended that there was a “Payment Verification System”, based on the Monthly Invoices Spreadsheet prepared by Fion[^457], “in place for TSM to verify that payments were made…and \[to\] monitor outstanding payments.[^458]

287    It was Fion’s evidence that she prepared the Monthly Invoices Spreadsheets using the invoices that Quah printed from ACCPAC and passed her. However, these spreadsheets did not contain any information as to whether the Company’s customers had in fact paid. They merely set out the amounts stated on the invoices. After preparing the spreadsheets, Fion would print them out and pass them to TSM. TSM would review the spreadsheets against “the figures or monthly diesel invoices” kept in a blue file. If he saw that some customers had pumped too much diesel, he would then check with Quah if these customers had already paid. If they had done so, Quah would strike the relevant entry off the spreadsheets. According to Fion, these spreadsheets were then placed on a desk in the Office. As and when customers paid, she or Quah (depending on who collected payment) would strike the relevant entry off the spreadsheets.[^459]

288    TSM did not deny the existence of these spreadsheets in cross-examination. It was his evidence that Fion prepared these spreadsheets for him “to see which customer owed how much every month.”[^460] He agreed that he therefore had a system for keeping track of which customers made payment every month through these spreadsheets.[^461] That said, he testified that he would “just look at who owe money and was the money paid.”[^462] He did this to ensure that customers did not owe the Company money for more than two or three months – for this would affect the Company’s ability to pay its diesel supplier. He also testified that he did not check the bank statements of the Company’s Account to ensure that cheques issued to the Company cleared. In this vein, I noted that TSM testified that he did not check the bank statements for the Company’s Account from 1 February 2010 to 31 December 2014[^463] on a monthly basis. It was his evidence that it was Quah who checked these statements. When asked to explain this practice (as Quah was not a director), he readily replied that “everything to do with accounts and correspondences \[Quah\] would be the one who handle it. She is the accounts.”[^464]

289    TSM’s attention was then drawn to the February 2010 bank statement, which showed a substantial deposit of $293,404.90. When asked if he was “concern\[ed\] about checking where this deposit came from”, he explained that he trusted Quah to review the Company’s bank statements as he focused on sourcing for business for the Company:[^465]

> A:    Because, usually, for these kind of correspondences, it’s not as if it’s a significantly large amount. **So, every month if the accounts or whatever that is right or wrong with regard to the accounts, I would hand it over to \[Quah\] to handle…them. I trust\[ed\] \[Quah\] to do so.**

> Q:    Mr Tan, you keep saying that you trust her, you trust her. So, what exactly is your role in the \[C\]ompany?

> A:    I would source for business outside.

> Q:    No, surely as a director, that cannot be the only role that you perform, right?

> A:    Because we are a very small company. I’m not a big company. So, as long as I can bring in business, then it would be alright.

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

290    TSM was also drawn to the May 2013 bank statement, which showed a “fast cheque deposit of $655,000” into the Company’s Account. When asked if he checked this statement to ensure that this cheque cleared, his testimony revealed the degree of trust he placed in Quah:[^466]

> Q:    Mr Tan, surely you would have checked your bank statements to make sure that this cheque cleared, right?

> **A:**      **I have never checked, because I trust\[ed\] \[Quah\] to do it.**

> Q:    Mr Tan, you’re saying even for such a big amount, you didn’t care whether the cheque cleared or not?

> **A:**      **I trusted her 100%. So, everything would be handled by her.**

> **Q:**      **Now, Mr Tan, I put it to you, every month when the bank account statements are sent to the company, you would have reviewed them.**

> **A:**      **I did not.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

291    TSM also gave evidence that showed that the effectiveness of the Monthly Invoices Spreadsheets as a means to keep track of whether customers paid depended on Quah. Key aspects of his evidence are set out in **Annex D** to these grounds.[^467] It was TSM’s evidence that he “only \[took\] a look at what \[Quah\] showed \[him\].”[^468] “\[I\]f \[Quah\] cancelled \[the entry on the spreadsheets\], that means payment was made. If \[Quah\] did not cancel \[the entry\], that means payment was not made.” As such, “\[i\]f what was given \[to him was\] wrong, \[he\] would not know.[^469] I also bore in mind Fion’s evidence that TSM _still_ had to check with Quah if customers had paid – notwithstanding his receipt of these spreadsheets from her.[^470] Indeed, TSM’s reliance on (and trust in) Quah was apparent when he was questioned on the audit verification issued to EDS.[^471]

292    In these circumstances, I did not agree with Counsel that there was a “Payment Verification System”, based on the Monthly Invoices Spreadsheet, “in place for TSM to verify that payments were made…and \[to\] monitor outstanding payments. The evidence showed that TSM trusted Quah to verify if customers did in fact make payment.

### Conclusion on conviction

293    Here, Quah accepted that she had collected _all_ of the Entrusted Monies (which belonged to the Company) on the Company’s behalf. The Entrusted Monies comprised payments made in cash and by way of cash cheques – modes of payment that Quah had also specifically sought from some of the Clients.[^472]

294    Quah did not dispute that she did not bank _any_ of the Entrusted Monies into the Company’s Account. The objective evidence showed that she had instead banked a large number of cash cheques (for payments due to the Company) into accounts that she solely controlled. Two of these accounts were also lines of credit, which were neither savings nor current accounts. One of these lines of credit had further been perpetually in overdraft. To explain why she banked the Company’s cash cheques into her accounts, she claimed that TSM had instructed her to do so and she had merely complied.

295    However, key aspects of Quah’s testimony on TSM’s supposed instructions were either contradicted or unsupported by other evidence. Her case also constantly evolved (even between days of trial), whenever she was confronted with contrary evidence. Indeed, her cross-examination was marked by numerous fresh claims, allegations, and afterthoughts[^473] – all of which were concocted to explain the glaring inconsistencies in her case, and to counteract evidence that showed that she did not pass the Entrusted Monies to TSM (as she claimed she did).[^474] Indeed, when pressed to explain why she raised so many new facts under cross-examination relating to TSM’s alleged instructions, on which her case hinged, her response – “nobody asked me” – spoke volumes about the veracity of her evidence:[^475]

> Q:    So let me re-summarise the proposition for you. Are you trying to tell me that it’s not dishonest if \[TSM\] gave you permission?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    But if \[TSM\] did not give you permission, it would be dishonest, you agree?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    So, whether \[TSM\] gave you permission is, in fact, a very important fact for your defence.

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    And in spite of that, you have raised all these new facts that were not put to \[TSM\].

> **A:**      **Nobody asked me.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

296    In my judgment, Quah was a thoroughly unreliable and untruthful witness. She failed to satisfactorily account for the Entrusted Monies (which she admitted she had collected on the Company’s behalf) or offer any plausible explanation for their loss. She did not even attempt, at trial, to account for more than a third of the Entrusted Monies.[^476]

297    The evidence further showed that Quah unilaterally manipulated the Company’s accounting records (which she _solely_ prepared) on ACCPAC (that _only_ she could access) to hide her misappropriation of the Entrusted Monies.[^477] This was further evidence of her dishonesty. To ensure her misappropriation was not detected, she also did not explain the contents and significance of the audit verifications she gave to the Directors to sign (which were based on the accounting records she had solely prepared).[^478] Indeed, she was initially successful in hiding her misappropriation over the course of the offending period – for the accounting irregularities were only uncovered when a special audit was performed by the Audit Firm after she left the Company.

298    Unlike Quah, I found TSM to be a credible witness. He testified simply, without hesitation, and in a straightforward manner. The evidence showed that he had reposed a significant degree of trust in Quah, and relied entirely on her to handle the Company’s accounts in its best interests.[^479] LEC and NES might not have known much about the Company’s affairs[^480], but they too had trusted Quah significantly. After all, Quah was the Company’s best-educated employee in a very small company, whose only other employees were the Directors[^481]; most proficient in the English language[^482]; computer literate; well versed in ACCPAC; and had been in charge of the Company’s accounts (and accounting records) since she joined the Company in 2000. In contrast, the Directors were not at all proficient in the English language[^483]; computer illiterate; and did not know how to use ACCPAC.[^484] They did not even know how to turn on a computer.

299    The totality of the evidence led me “inevitably and inexorably” to conclude that Quah did dishonestly misappropriate the Entrusted Monies on her own volition (and not pursuant to any instructions from TSM). She knew that wrongful loss would result from her activities[^485], which were done with the intention of causing wrongful loss to the Company (which was wrongfully deprived of the Entrusted Monies, to which it was legally entitled). There were no other alternative inferences or conclusion reasonably possible, other than she had committed the offences. I thus convicted her on all charges.

## Decision on sentence

300    An offender who commits criminal breach of trust by a servant “shall be punished with imprisonment for a term which may extend to 15 years, and shall also be liable to fine”. Imprisonment is thus mandatory. That said, a District Court is only empowered to pass up to 10 years’ imprisonment per charge.[^486] Subject to this limit on sentencing jurisdiction, the capping of the sentence at 10 years’ imprisonment is not a relevant sentencing consideration.[^487]

### Sentences sought by parties

301    The sentences sought by the parties in respect of _each_ charge did not differ widely. The key difference lay in the _aggregate_ sentence sought:[^488]

> (a)     the Prosecution sought “at least” 84 months’ imprisonment, _via_ an order that _three_ of the _heaviest_ sentences run consecutively.

> (b)     Counsel sought between 48 to 60 months’ imprisonment, _via_ an order that the sentences for only _two_ charges run consecutively.

302    The table on the following page sets out the sentences sought and those I eventually imposed (the shaded rows indicate those to be run consecutively).

<table align="left" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="Judg-2-tblr" frame="all" pgwide="1"><colgroup><col width="23.2%"> <col width="27.18%"> <col width="32.6%"> <col width="17.02%"> class="Table-Para-1"><b>Total term sought/imposed</b></p></td><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">4 months’ (at least)</p></td><td align="left" ### Reasons for sentences imposed

303    Quah was a “multiple offender” (_viz_, an offender to be sentenced for convictions on multiple offences).[^489] I therefore applied the three-stage framework in _PP v Raveen Balakrishnan_[^490] (“_Raveen_”) in sentencing her.

#### Stage 1: Appropriate sentence for each charge

304    The “first stage of the sentencing analysis is…to consider the appropriate sentence for each offence. This may be done in a number of ways, including by application of a sentencing framework or benchmark, or by analogy with precedents. In arriving at the individual sentences, the sentencing court will generally have to consider the relevant aggravating and mitigating factors that bear upon each discrete sentence”[^491].

##### General sentencing considerations

305    Retribution and general deterrence are key sentencing principles in cases of criminal breach of trust.[^492] The general sentencing considerations in such cases were also discussed in _PP v Lam Leng Hung_[^493], in the following terms:

> 367    We accept the general proposition that **in respect of property offences, including that of CBT, the starting consideration ought to be the value of the property misappropriated**. This principle has been reiterated in many cases. In _Wong Kai Chuen Philip v PP_ <span class="citation">\[1990\] 2 SLR(R) 361</span> (“_Philip Wong_”), Chan Sek Keong J observed (at \[18\]) that **in an offence like CBT, “it \[was\] a matter of common sense that, all other things being equal, the larger the amount dishonestly misappropriated the greater the culpability of the offender and the more severe the sentence of the court”.** More recently, in the case of _Idya Nurhazlyn bte Ahmad Khir v PP_ <span class="citation">\[2014\] 1 SLR 756</span>, Sundaresh Menon CJ stated in a similar vein that the “primary yardstick” involved in sentencing for an offence of cheating under s 417 of the Penal Code would often be the “value of the property involved” (at \[48\]).

> 368     **While the value of the property misappropriated ought ordinarily to be the starting point for the analysis of the appropriate sentence, it also bears emphasis that the court’s discretion in sentencing is never restricted to the application of a mathematical formula based on the amount in question** (see, for example, the observations of Yong CJ in _Amir Hamzah bin Berang Kuty v PP_ <span class="citation">\[2003\] 1 SLR(R) 617</span> at \[60\]). As Lee Seiu Kin J observed in _Tan Cheng Yew_ (\[96\] supra) at \[184\], **it is common sense that sentences for CBT offences do not bear a relationship of linear proportionality with the sums involved. The appropriate sentence to be imposed must be arrived at after having regard not just to the amounts in question, but also to the totality of the circumstances, particularly the specific facts of the case.**

> 369    In this connection, we find the following non-exhaustive factors set out by the English Court of Appeal in _R v John Barrick_ (1985) 81 Cr App R 78 at 81–82 relevant. While these factors were set out in the context of the offences of theft and fraud, we find them to be also relevant in the context of the offence of CBT. These factors were also cited in _Philip Wong_ at \[25\]. They include (**a) the quality and degree of trust reposed in the offender, which would encompass a consideration of his rank; (b) the period over which the act was perpetrated;** (c) the use to which the money or property that was dishonestly taken was put; (d) the effect upon the victim; and (e) the impact of the offences on the public and public confidence.

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

306    With these considerations in mind, I now review the aggravating and mitigating factors in this case. Two reported sentencing precedents are then examined. I conclude this section with my reasons for the individual sentences imposed.

##### Aggravating factors

307    The facts disclosed the following aggravating factors:

> (a)     Quah breached the very significant degree of trust reposed in her.

> (b)     Quah manipulated the accounting records to avoid detection.

> (c)     Quah’s offending was premediated and planned.

308    I now discuss these factors. The second and third factors, in particular, overlapped considerably as they shared a common normative substance. To avoid double counting and putting excessive weight on them, I considered them holistically and applied only a _single_ uplift in sentence.

Breach of significant degree of trust

309    I did not treat the fact that Quah was employed as a servant of the Company as aggravating, because this was an ingredient of the offence.[^494] In addition, Quah might have abused the trust reposed in her by the Company when she dishonestly misappropriated the Entrusted Monies. But, I did not treat the abuse of trust, in itself, as aggravating – since an abuse of trust is a factor inherent in every offence of criminal breach of trust.[^495]_Kavitha d/o Mailvaganam v PP_[^496] (“_Kavitha_”) is useful on both points. The offender there was a customer service officer. In this role, she was tasked by her employer (“JPB”) to collect payments from customers and to record them in JPB’s internal accounting system. Over the course of five months, she misappropriated cash payments that she received from different customers by recording:

> (a)     in JPB’s internal accounting system a lower amount paid by the customers, and misappropriating the difference; and

> (b)     other payments made _via_ cheque or NETS under the name of cash-paying customers, and misappropriating the entire payment.

310    In sentencing, the District Court regarded the offender’s offence as aggravated as she “was placed in a high position of trust” as a customer service officer”. The High Court disagreed, reasoning as follows:

> 18    …**It is true that the quality and degree of trust reposed in the offender, including his position in the relevant establishment, is a factor to be taken into account when determining the sentence for an offence under s 408 of the Penal Code** (_Wong Kai Chuen Philip v PP_ <span class="citation">\[1990\] 2 SLR(R) 361</span> at \[25\]). **In the present case, however, the \[offender\] was a customer service officer and there was no evidence to suggest that she was entrusted with any special level of responsibility in JPB or was in any elevated position of trust. Although her job scope as a customer service officer included having to collect payments made by customers and record them in JPB’s internal accounting system, this was a role which would be given to any sales personnel within a company**. In addition, the fact that the appellant had abused this responsibility was already reflected in the charge itself which was for committing criminal breach of trust _as a clerk or servant_ – a more serious offence than a criminal breach of trust _simpliciter_ (see _Lim Ying Ying Luciana v PP_ <span class="citation">\[2016\] 4 SLR 1220</span> (“_Luciana Lim_”) at \[67\]). Hence, the appellant’s abuse of the trust reposed in her should not have been considered by the DJ as a separate aggravating factor.

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

311    In this case, the Prosecution argued that the sentences imposed should reflect the fact that Quah had “breached the very significant degree of trust reposed in her”:

> 9    \[Quah\] was a manager of \[the Company\] for more than 14 years, between 2 May 2000 and 25 August 2014. **She oversaw \[the Company’s\] accounts**…**and controlled its data entry**. \[Quah\] was in fact more highly educated than \[the Company’s Directors\]; she has A-Level qualifications, is able to read, write and speak English fluently, and was proficient in \[the Company’s\] accounting software \[_ie_, ACCPAC\].

> 10    Against this backdrop, **\[Quah\] was deeply trusted by TSM to appropriately manage \[the Company’s\] accounts**. Her position gave her dominion and control over a significant portion of \[the Company’s\] funds. **In fact, she was so trusted that TSM effectively ceased overseeing her work after several years** \[as shown by the evidence of TSM\]:

>> Q:    So, can you just confirm that \[Quah\] did in fact report to you?

>> A:    Initially for a few years.

>> Q:    And do you oversee her work?

>> A:    Initially I did for quite some years, but later because I have heart problem and I underwent surgery. I thought that she was quite good after working for me for a few years. So after 7 to 8 years and because of my heart problems, I just let her do her work.

>> \---

>> Q:    I’ll move on. Who else, if anyone, worked with \[Quah\] on \[the Company’s\] accounts between 2009 and 2014?

>> A:    No, she take over everything and she was in charge of everything.

>> Q:    Did anyone report to \[Quah\]?

>> A:    I don’t think so, it’s just that Fion would assist her.

>> …

>> Q:    So, coming back to my question Mr Tan, do you know the information ACCPAC records?

>> A:    I do not know, I only know that all the accounts could be inside.

>> Q:    So, do you double check \[Quah’s\] entries on ACCPAC?

>> A:    I do not know how to. How could I check?

>> Q:    Do you know how to use a computer?

>> A:    No.

> 11     **\[Quah\] was the sole person \[in the Company\] with full oversight of \[the Company’s\] accounts** \[who\] exploited the high degree of trust reposed in her to \[its\] significant detriment…

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

312    I agreed that the sentences imposed should reflect the quality and degree of trust reposed in Quah, including her position in the Company.[^497] In this vein, I bore in mind that “in determining the level of trust reposed in a person, the court looks to substance and not to form; thus a person with a humble title may occupy a high position of trust”.[^498]

313    The evidence showed that Quah was the _only_ employee in a very small company (whose only other employees were the Directors) who performed _all_ of the following duties (with _negligible_ oversight):

> (a)     liaising with the Company’s customers on payment;

> (b)     collecting payments from the Company’s customers (including _cash_ payments) on the Company’s behalf;

> (c)     preparing the Company’ accounting records on ACCPAC, to which only she had access (as the Prosecution noted, Quah “controlled the \[Company’s\] data entry” on ACCPAC[^499]); and

> (d)     liaising with the Audit Firm on its general audits of the Company and the Company’s accounting records (including allaying any concerns the Audit Firm might have had with these records after its audits).

314    The degree of trust the Directors (the three remaining employees in the Company) reposed in Quah was also significant. This stemmed from her being the _only_ employee in the Company who:

> (a)     was proficient in the English language (the language used in ACCPAC and all the accounting documents);

> (b)     was computer literate;

> (c)     knew how to use ACCPAC; and

> (d)     dealt with the Company’s accounts

315    Indeed, the degree of trust the Directors had in Quah was so great that they blindly signed the audit verifications she handed them (which contained false information). LEC’s and NEC’s limited concern about the Company’s affairs was also significant – for they were the only two other persons in the Company (apart from TSM) who could have monitored Quah’s work (but clearly did not). TSM, in particular, reposed a significant degree of trust in Quah. Among other things, he trusted Quah to:

> (a)     monitor if customers had already paid the Company;[^500]

> (b)     write the sums on his personal cheques, for him to return to the Company the cash payments he had received from Quah[^501]; and

> (c)     review the Company’s bank account statements and the sums deposited into the Company’s Account[^502].

316    Hence, notwithstanding Quah’s nondescript title of “Manager”, she plainly occupied an elevated position of trust in the Company.

Accounting records manipulated to avoid detection

317    “A sophisticated offence replete with carefully orchestrated efforts and steps to avoid detection is an aggravating factor”.[^503] That said, “a criminal scheme may be said to be “sophisticated” and “carefully orchestrated” even if it did not involve the use of complicated technology and was carried out by a single operator working alone, rather than a syndicate. In all cases, what is critical is not the intricacy of the criminal enterprise _per se_, but whether there were careful steps taken to avoid detection”.[^504]

318    Here, Quah had manipulated the accounting records to hide her misappropriation of the Entrusted Monies”.[^505] Her failure to explain the audit verifications she gave to the Directors to sign was also no accident. It was part of her efforts to avoid detection. These verifications were based on the accounting records she solely prepared and manipulated. Had she properly explained them to the Directors, her manipulation of these records would have been uncovered. That said, I was mindful not to give excessive weight to this factor. This was because the evidence did not show that the offences were _replete_ with carefully orchestrated efforts to avoid detection.

Premeditated & planned offending

319    General deterrence aside[^506], specific deterrence is appropriate where an offence is premediated. Specific deterrence was applicable here because Quah’s offences were premeditated _and_ planned. Here, the offences were not committed impulsively because of a momentary lapse of judgment. They were all premediated and planned. The presence of planning and premeditation evinces a considered commitment towards law breaking, and reflects greater criminality and culpability.[^507] That said, I was mindful:

> (a)     that “deterrence must always be tempered by proportionality in relation to the severity of the offences committed as well as by the moral and legal culpability of the offender.”[^508]

> (b)     not to attach excessive weight to this factor, because:

>> (i)       it is inherent in the offence of criminal breach of trust that there would have been _some_ premeditation and planning. This is not usually an offence that happens spontaneously. It is the _degree_ of premeditation and planning that is more relevant in sentencing.

>> (ii)       the premeditation and planning in this case was evident largely from the active steps Quah took to avoid detection.[^509]

##### Mitigating factors of limited weight

320    Counsel urged the court to accept the following “mitigating factors”:[^510]

> (a)     Quah had “no prior antecedents”.

> (b)     Quah was “cooperative with the police during their investigations…evident from the fact that she provided 12 statements to the police. These included 7 \[investigation\] statements, which she provided over the period from 21 April 2015 to 2 January 2018, _ie_, a period of almost 3 years”.

> (c)     Quah “acted reasonably in her Defence, with a view to narrow the disputed issues \[as\] reflected in her readiness to agree on an Agreed Statement of Facts and a Bundle of Agreed Documents”.

321    I did not find these factors particularly compelling. I now elaborate.

Quah was not a first-time offender

322    I was aware that Quah had no antecedents[^511], but gave limited weight to this factor. This was in light of the five charges against her, which encompassed 168 distinct instances of misappropriation over nearly five years.[^512] She was thus not entitled to be treated as a first-time offender – a principle recognised in _Chen Weixiong Jerriek v PP_:[^513]

> 15     **The appellant could not be regarded as a “first offender” in any sense of the phrase. While he had no antecedents in the sense of prior convictions, he had pleaded guilty to seven charges** of robbery with common intention, robbery with hurt with common intention and voluntarily causing hurt by means of a dangerous weapon. **Further, no less than 38 other charges were taken into consideration for the purpose of sentencing** – these comprised 32 charges for offences of robbery with common intention and six charges for offences of robbery with hurt with common intention. **The only reason the appellant had no prior convictions was because the law had not yet caught up with him for his past misdeeds.**

> …

> 17    …it is the prerogative of this court **to refuse to consider as a first time offender anyone who has been charged with multiple offences, even if he has no prior convictions. I would venture further to hold that in any case, the courts in general should be extremely reluctant to regard such persons as first time offenders.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

323    Evidence of good character (in that Quah had no criminal antecedents) was also of little mitigating weight in this case, which involved an abuse of trust. This was because Quah must have been placed in a position of trust by virtue of her previous good character. But, she subsequently abused this trust.[^514]

Quah’s “cooperation” with authorities not mitigating

324    The mere fact that Quah provided 12 statements to the police[^515] was not, in itself, a mitigating factor, for which she deserved credit.

325    I deal first with the seven investigation statements recorded from Quah. This was done pursuant to s 22 of the Criminal Procedure Code (“the CPC”).[^516] Section 22(1) empowers a police officer conducting an investigation under Part IV of the CPC to record a statement from “any person who appears to be acquainted with any of the facts and circumstances of \[a\] case”. Under s 22(2) of the CPC, the “person examined shall be bound to state truly what \[she\] knows of the facts and circumstances of the case, except that \[she\] need not say anything that might expose \[her\] to a criminal charge, penalty or forfeiture”. Quah was hence “bound to state truly what \[she knew\] of the facts and circumstances of the case” save for “anything that \[would have exposed her\] to a criminal charge, penalty or forfeiture”. As such, Quah’s “mere presence and compliance with \[the\] statement-recording procedures \[in the CPC cannot, in itself, be said to be\] indicative of \[any\] cooperativeness”[^517] on her part. Indeed, had she intentionally omitted to furnish information or refused to answer any question demanded of her (save for “anything that \[would have exposed her\] to a criminal charge, penalty or forfeiture”), she could have been prosecuted under ss 176 or 179 of the Penal Code.[^518]

326    In any event, Quah was in fact _not_ cooperative – for she had maintained her claim in her statements that she handed the Entrusted Monies to TSM.[^519]

327    Quah’s cautioned statements also bear mention. These were recorded from her _after_ her investigation statements. There, she _denied_ all of the charges and reiterated that she had been acting on TSM’s instructions.[^520]

328    I did not discern any remorse from Quah in any of her statements, for which she deserved any credit. That said, I did not attach any weight to the fact that Quah claimed trial. She was entitled to exercise her constitutional right to plead not guilty and to claim trial.[^521] However, as her counsel acknowledged, “an accused person who pleads guilty would typically be entitled to some sentencing discount…to take into account the accused’s plea of guilt as a mitigating factor reflecting \[her\] remorse”.[^522] Given that Quah had not pleaded guilty, she was thus not entitled to any sentencing discount.[^523]

Quah’s conduct of defence not particularly mitigating

329    Quah did “agree on an Agreed Statement of Facts and a Bundle of Agreed Documents”. These certainly “narrow\[ed\] the disputed issues”.[^524] That said, I did not attach significant weight to this factor. This was because the “Bundle of Agreed Documents” comprised documents which:

> (a)     the Prosecution would not have had much difficulty in proving:

>> (i)       bank statements (**AB1** to **AB7**);

>> (ii)       documents prepared by Quah using ACCPAC and extracted from ACCPAC: the Clients’ Transactions Records (**AB8** to **AB13**), the Company’s general ledgers (**AB38** and **AB39**), and invoices issued to the Clients (**AB32** to **AB37**); and

>> (iii)       cash cheques issued by the Clients, with Quah’s name and bank account numbers on the back (**AB14** to **AB18**).

> (b)     were consistent with Quah’s case (and so were actually adduced in support of her case):

>> (i)       investigation statements (**AB20** to **AB26**); and

>> (ii)       cautioned statements (**AB27** to **AB31**).

> (c)     were neutral, such as Quah’s resignation letter (**AB19**).

330    The “Agreed Statement of Facts” related largely to the documents in the “Agreed Bundle of Documents”. I noted that Quah did additionally agree that she had collected _part_ of the Entrusted Monies from the Clients during the offending period.[^525]. That said, she had already reflected these sums as having been received in the Clients’ Customer Transactions Records. The bulk of this part of the Entrusted Monies also related to payments for which the Prosecution had obtained images of the relevant cheques (on which Quah had written her name and account numbers). In addition, she was “not prepared to accept that she had received all of the \[Entrusted Monies\] (as \[was\] her right) and only eventually conceded this under cross-examination”.[^526]

No restitution/recovery of Entrusted Monies

331    There was neither restitution nor recovery of any of the Entrusted Monies. There was hence no basis for any attenuation in sentencing, on account of remorse on Quah’s part or a reduction in the harm caused by her offences.

##### Reported sentencing precedents

332    As indicated[^527], the sentences sought by the parties in respect of _each_ charge did not differ widely. The individual sentences sought by the Prosecution ranged from 20 to 30 months’ imprisonment, while those sought by Counsel ranged from 24 to 30 months’ imprisonment. The key difference between the parties lay in the number of sentences that should be run consecutively (and, hence the total sentence). Against this backdrop, I discuss two reported sentencing precedents that I found especially useful in calibrating the individual sentences. Three points, however, bear mention at the outset. First, I did not find Counsel’s reliance on _PP v Bahurudeen Kuthpudeen_ (“_Bahurudeen_”)[^528] useful. Counsel’s submissions were in the following terms:

> 14.    In \[_Bahurudeen_\], the accused pleaded guilty to 1 charge under s 408 of the Penal Code and 1 charge under the Corruption, Drug Trafficking and Other Serious Crimes (Confiscation of Benefits) Act (“CDSA”). **The s 408 charge involved a sum of S$469,900/-, and it was misappropriated on one occasion. The accused did not make any restitution.** At \[14\] of _Bahurudeen_, the learned District Judge referred to a summary of s 408 precedents. **The court considered that the accused’s plea of guilt had little mitigating value (because he actually fled to overseas before being arrested and detained). Eventually a sentence of 42 months’ imprisonment was imposed on the accused for the s 408 charge.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

333    It was not entirely clear if Counsel was relying on _Bahurudeen_ in support of his position on the individual sentences or the aggregate sentence. This was because the sum stated in the single proceeded s 408 Penal Code charge in _Bahurudeen_ was $469,900 – _far_ greater than the sums stated in any of the individual charges against Quah.[^529] “Generally, the larger the amount misappropriated, the more severe the punishment that should be meted out”.[^530] The converse must also be true. _Bahurudeen_ was hence not useful in calibrating the individual sentences in this case. In any event, while Counsel was correct that the offender in _Bahurudeen_ did not make any restitution, he failed to note the fact that the _bulk_ of the misappropriated sum – amounting to more than $458,600 – was recovered. This was clear from the following paragraphs in the statement of facts tendered in _Bahurudeen_:

> 12.    The accused was eventually detained at the border of Kedah, when attempting to leave Malaysia for Thailand. **Cash amounting to S$23,652/-, Malaysia Ringgit 701/- and Brunei $1/- was found in his possession and seized. The moneys seized from the accused are part of the sum he misappropriated.**

> 13.    According to the accused, he had met a friend of his in Malaysia and handed him approximately S$430,000 of the money he had misappropriated. The accused also claimed that he had informed his friend that the money belonged to the accused and a few friends, and that he would contact him in two days’ time to collect the money. Eventually, the accused’s friend came to know about the accused’s arrest. The accused’s friend’s brother then made arrangements to meet with the complainant to return him the money. T**he complainant met the accused’s friend’s brother together with other individuals in Malaysia and was returned a sum of S$435,000**, of which he gave $3,400 as a reward to them for their assistance and honesty.

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

334    The recovery of this sum did not amount to voluntary restitution. It was also “solely due to the efforts of third parties, namely the authorities involved in \[the offender’s\] detention at the Kedah border, and the \[offender’s\] friend’s brother and others who had returned the money to the \[victim\]”.[^531] That said, the fact that “a significant amount of money was finally recovered” was _still_ given _some_ weight (though not as a mitigating factor in the offender’s favour). This was because the recovery of such a significant sum (amounting to more than 97% of the sum stated in the charge) had the effect of significantly reducing the measure of loss to the victim (or the harm caused by the offence).[^532] It would have been wrong, in principle, to punish the offender in _Bahurudeen_ as if the measure of loss to the victim (and the harm caused) was in fact $469,900. After all, the severity of an offence must be assessed by having regard to the principal parameters of harm caused and the offender’s culpability.[^533]

335    In addition, Counsel was not entirely correct that the offender’s plea of guilt had “little mitigating value”.[^534]_Some_ weight was given to the offender’s guilty plea, as is evident from the following paragraph in _Bahurudeen_:

> 38    Further, while the accused did ultimately plead guilty, I noted that in light of his detention at the Kedah border with part of the tainted sum, the fact that he had fled Singapore soon after the commission of the misappropriation offence, and was also attempting to flee further to Thailand, as well as the fact that his offences had already been uncovered by the time he was detained, meant that there was clearly overwhelming evidence to support his conviction. In this regard, his plea of guilt may have little or no mitigating value. This is consistent with the decision of the Court of Appeal in _Fu Foo Tong and others v Public Prosecutor_ <span class="citation">\[1995\] 1 SLR(R) 1</span>, where the Court made clear at \[12\] that “A plea of guilt can be of no mitigating value, for example, when the evidence overwhelmingly supports a conviction.” **That said, I recognised and accepted that his plea of guilt had at least saved time and public resources that might otherwise have been expended if there was a trial, and it also dispensed with the need for witnesses to testify (see** **_Ng Kean Meng Terence v Public Prosecutor_** **<span class="citation">\[2017\] 2 SLR 449</span>). To that extent, I did give some weight to his guilty plea.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

336     Second, I was mindful that while the punishments imposed for offences involving criminal breach of trust ought to increase in severity with the amounts misappropriated, the sentences do not bear a relationship of liner proportionality with the sums involved. This point was expressly recognised by the High Court in _Lim Ying Ying Luciana v PP_[^535] (“_Luciana Lim_”), in the following terms:[^536]

> **_Sentencing precedents_**

> 68    As far as the precedents were concerned, the Prosecution cautioned against reliance on the older cases and submitted that there had been a “clear upward trend in the sentences for such offences in recent years”. They drew my attention to the following three decisions of the District Court – _PP v Lee Han Boon Adrian_ DAC 911016/2014, _PP v Koh Mui Hoong_ DAC 500714/2013, _PP v Yeo Kay Keng Matthew_ <span class="citation">\[2011\] SGDC 425</span> (“_Matthew Yeo_”) – all of which involved the misappropriation of between $1m and $2m and in respect of which sentences of between five and six years’ imprisonment were imposed. Extrapolating from this, they submitted that the starting point of nine years’ imprisonment arrived at by the District Judge was too low and that a starting point of between ten and 12 years’ imprisonment ought to have been ordered instead. With respect, I could not agree with this.

> 69     **There is no question that the amount of money misappropriated is an important sentencing consideration. Generally, the larger the amount misappropriated, the more severe the punishment that should be meted out. However, it has been emphasised in past cases that while the punishments imposed for CBT offences ought to increase in severity with the amounts misappropriated, the sentences “do not bear a relationship of linear proportionality with the sums involved” (see** **_PP v Tan Cheng Yew_** **<span class="citation">\[2013\] 1 SLR 1095</span> (“Tan Cheng Yew”) at \[184\]). The fact that proportionately higher punishments were imposed for offences involving smaller sums of money does not, without more, imply that the District Judge had selected an inappropriate starting point or that the eventual sentence she arrived at was manifestly inadequate.** I also did not think it possible to derive a sentencing range from such a small sample size (particularly considering the circumspection with which unreported decisions are generally viewed: see _Luong Thi Trang Hoang Kathleen v PP_ <span class="citation">\[2010\] 1 SLR 707</span> at \[21\]).

> \[emphasis in bold italics in original; emphasis added in bold\]

337     Third, in calibrating the individual sentences in this case, I also relied on decisions where the relevant charges had been framed under the 1985 revised edition of the Penal Code (“the 1985 Penal Code”).[^537] Under this earlier edition of the Penal Code, the offence of criminal breach of trust by a servant was punishable with mandatory imprisonment of up to 7 years (only). This was _less than half_ the maximum of 15 years’ imprisonment that Quah was liable for in respect of each of the five charges she faced under the current edition of the Penal Code. I set out this difference in the table below:

<table align="left" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="Judg-2-tblr" frame="all" pgwide="1"><colgroup><col width="29.8%"> <col width="46.84%"> <col width="23.36%"> </colgroup><tbody><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Offence committed</b></p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Maximum term prescribed</b></p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Remarks</b></p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">Before 1 February 2008</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">7 years’ imprisonment</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">1985 Penal Code</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">After 1 February 2008</p></td><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">15 years’ imprisonment</p></td><td align="left" class="" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">Quah’s charges</p></td></tr></tbody></table>

  
  

338    That said, the amendments to the Penal Code that came into effect in 2008 did not mean that sentencing precedents for offences under s 408 of the 1985 Penal Code are irrelevant. They continue to serve as a good guide. These amendments – which more than doubled the maximum imprisonment term for criminal breach of trust as a servant – also did not mean that Parliament intended for the imprisonment terms for criminal breach of trust as a servant to “automatically” go up regardless of the facts of the case. These two points were evident during the second reading of the Penal Code (Amendment) Bill:[^538]

> …we must leave it to the courts to mete out punishment. … **This is the point that I want to address when Mr Lim Biow Chuan asks whether what we have done will lead automatically to fines or punishments going up. I do not think so.** He has mentioned, for example, the benchmarks, the sentencing guidelines, that the courts have. **I think the guidelines will continue. It does not mean that automatically when the maximum punishment is raised, the punishment will go up.** Because every punishment must depend on the facts of the case. And I think the new Chief Justice has mentioned that the punishment should fit the crime as well as the offender…

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

339    However, I was also mindful of the need for consistency with legislative intent given the increase in the punishment for the offences that Quah had committed. This was recognised in _Lam Leng Hung_ in the following terms:

> 386    The increase in the maximum imprisonment term for the offence of CBT in the 2008 revised edition of the Penal Code was the result of a comprehensive review of the penalty regime across a number of offences under the Penal Code carried out by the Ministry of Home Affairs, the Attorney-General’s Chambers, the Ministry of Law and other government agencies. During the second reading of the Penal Code (Amendment) Bill 2007 (No 38 of 2007), the Senior Minister of State for Home Affairs identified four principles by which the review of the penalties for the various offences was undertaken. These principles were as follows: (a) the type and quantum of punishment should provide sufficient flexibility to the courts to mete out an appropriate sentence in each case; (b) the prevalence of the offence; (c) the proportionality of the penalty to an offence, taking into account the seriousness of the offence; and (d) the relativity in punishment between related offences (see _Singapore Parliamentary Debates, Official Report_ (22 October 2007) vol 83 col 2201 (Senior Minister of State for Home Affairs Assoc Prof Ho Peng Kee)).

> 387    It is **clear from the tenor of the Parliamentary Debates on the amendments that where Parliament increased the maximum sentence imposable for a particular offence, this signified that Parliament viewed the offence as more serious and therefore requiring stiffer punishments in line with current societal trends and circumstances** (see also Singapore Parliamentary Debates, Official Report (23 October 2007) vol 83 cols 2425–2444 (Senior Minister of State for Home Affairs Assoc Prof Ho Peng Kee)). This is also consistent with the observations in _Mehra Radhika v PP_ <span class="citation">\[2015\] 1 SLR 96</span> where Menon CJ said as follows (at \[27\]):

>> … As a generally operative background factor, if Parliament has increased the punishment for an offence on the basis that the mischief in question was becoming more serious and needed to be arrested … the courts would not be acting in concert with the legislative intent if they fail to have regard to this in developing the appropriate sentencing framework or if they nonetheless err on the side of leniency in sentencing.

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

340    I now discuss the two sentencing precedents I found especially useful.

Tan Kiang Chua Destry

341    The offender in _PP v Tan Kiang Chua Destry_ (“_Destry Tan_”)[^539] was an International Marketing Vice President of a company that operated casinos. His duties included carrying out sales, marketing, organising events, and collecting debts owed to the company by its clients. Between August 2005 and June 2006 (_viz_., a period almost 11 months), he misappropriated a total of $214,139.20.

342    The offender had an agreement with a client of the company, in which the client was to repay a debt owed to the company in instalments. The client paid these instalments to the company through the offender – who was to then remit the sums received into the company’s account. However, the offender only remitted part of the monies he received and kept the remainder (totalling $214,139.20) for himself.

343    The offender’s misappropriation came to light when the client was refused a cheque cashing facility in a casino because he had outstanding debts with the company. The client then informed the company that he had already repaid his debt (through the offender) and thus should not have been recorded as still owing it money. The company conducted an investigation into these claims, and discovered that the sums the client claimed to have paid to the company (through the offender) did not tally with its accounts. The offender then confessed his misdeeds to the company. He had used the misappropriated monies to pay off his gambling debts and for personal expenses. He made _full_ restitution to the company.

344    The offender had no prior antecedents. He pleaded guilty to a single charge under s 408 of the 1985 Penal Code. A second charge, also under s 408 of the 1985 Penal Code but involving a much lower sum of $20,225.39, was considered in sentencing (or “TIC”). He was sentenced as follows:

<table align="left" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="Judg-2-tblr" frame="all" pgwide="1"><colgroup><col width="30.54%"> <col width="53.8%"> <col width="15.66%"> </colgroup><tbody><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Sum misappropriated</b></p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Imprisonment</b></p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Remarks</b></p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$214,139.20</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">24 months’</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">-</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$20,225.39</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">-</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">TIC</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1"><b>$234,364.59 (total)</b></p></td><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1"><b>24 months’ imprisonment (total sentence)</b></p></td><td align="left" class="" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1"><b>-</b></p></td></tr></tbody></table>

  
  

345    In sentencing the offender, the District Court took into account the following:

> (a)     The offender’s use of the client’s instalments was “not on the spur of the moment \[but\] done with premeditation.[^540]

> (b)     Given the offender’s “senior position” as the International Marketing Vice President in the company, he was in a “high position” of trust.[^541]

> (c)     The total sum misappropriated (_viz_., $234,264.59) was substantial, and the sentence imposed should reflect this.[^542]

> (d)     The offender had “caused the \[client\] embarrassment” when the company refused to allow him the use a cheque cashing facility because he apparently had outstanding debts with the company.[^543]

> (e)     The offence committed “would undermine the confidence of the casino’s customers.[^544]

> (f)     The offender had used the misappropriated monies “to settle his gambling debts at the expense of his clients’ and employers’ interest”.[^545]

346    The District Court noted that it would have imposed a sentence of 36 months’ imprisonment but for the full restitution made. Given the offender’s full restitution, the court gave a “one third reduction” and imposed a sentence of 24 months’ imprisonment instead.[^546]The High Court dismissed the offender’s appeal against sentence.[^547]

Xie Ming Liang

347    The offender in _PP v Xie Ming Liang_[^548] was a Marketing and Operations Manager in a company. His duties included collecting payments from clients, which he had to hand over to the company. He continued to be entrusted with this task after he was promoted to Deputy General Manager in 2008. He faced seven charges, for misappropriating a total of $984,360.60 between 1 March 2004 and 24 December 2009. He spent all of the monies misappropriated on his gambling and other personal debts. He made restitution of $7,000.

348    The offender’s misdeeds were in fact discovered sometime in 2007 by the Managing Director of the company, who gave him a chance and even treated some of the monies taken as a “loan” from the company that the offender was to repay. However, the offender continued misappropriating monies from the Company. The company finally made a police report in December 2010.

349    The offender pleaded guilty to three charges. The remaining four charges were considered in sentencing. The charges were punishable under either the 1985 Penal Code or the Penal Code. The key details of all seven charges are set out in the table below:

![]([2020] SGDC 0066_Image/[2020] SGDC 0066_image3.png)

350    In sentencing the offender, the District Judge reasoned as follows:

> 9     **The Accused pleaded guilty and had only one previous conviction for a minor, dissimilar offence. However, where an offender faces multiple charges, even if he has no previous convictions, the Court should be extremely reluctant to regard him as a first offender**…**Given that it would have been clear from the clients’ evidence that he had collected the money, and he had already admitted to \[the Managing Director\] that he had pocketed the money, the case against him was strong. His guilty plea was thus of limited weight**. It is also clear that in a case of criminal breach of trust, **the larger the amount dishonestly misappropriated, the greater the culpability of the offender and the more severe the sentence** of the Court…

> …

> 11    In _Francis Yeo Eng Wah v PP_ (MA 179/97)[^549], the offender, a sales executive, pleaded guilty to one charge under section 408 of the Penal Code, for misappropriating $116,671.40 over the course of a year. The money had been collected by him from customers. No restitution was made. He was a first offender and was sentenced to two years’ imprisonment.

> 12    In _Moo Meng Kwong v PP_ (MA 130/98, unreported), the offender, the chief cashier of a bank, pleaded guilty to misappropriating S$514,000 and US$35,000. Some of this money was derived from him selling travellers’ cheques which he received in the course of his work and pocketing the proceeds, while others were taken from the bank vault to which he had access. \[All the monies misappropriated were used to settle his debts, most of which arose from investments in shares\]. **He made full restitution and was a first offender**. He was sentenced to three years’ imprisonment.[^550] This was affirmed on appeal.

> …

> 15    In considering the appropriate sentence to impose on the Accused, I noted that his **offences spanned a period of nearly six years.** He was **found out by his employers and given a chance, but did not seize that opportunity to make things right. Instead, in a total lack of contrition, he took advantage of \[the Managing Director’s\] generosity and carried on committing further offences.** His was a **prolonged course of criminal conduct which could not be attributed to a one-off moment of folly or lapse of judgment**. The **restitution made was hardly an appreciable amount (less than 1%) in comparison to what was taken**. Worst was the fact that his **debts were incurred due to gambling, instead of a more worthy cause such as, eg, helping a family member to finance medical treatment**. I thus found **few mitigating factors in his favour.** The **fact that he made no attempt to conceal his conduct was neutral at best, and could not be regarded as a mitigating factor, since it also demonstrated his brazenness.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

351    The offender was then sentenced as follows:

![]([2020] SGDC 0066_Image/[2020] SGDC 0066_image4.png)

352    The High Court dismissed the offender’s appeal against sentence.[^551]

##### Appropriate sentence for each charge

353    The table below lists the individual sentences sought by parties, and those I eventually imposed (in ascending order of the sums misappropriated):

<table align="left" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="Judg-2-tblr" frame="all" pgwide="1"><colgroup><col width="23.2%"> <col width="27.18%"> <col width="24.08%"> <col width="25.54%"> </colgroup><tbody><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">&nbsp;</p></td><td align="left" class="b" colspan="3" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p
