align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$169,974.60</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">23 months’ (at least)</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">24-30 months’</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">27 months’</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$174,166.12</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">25 months’ (at least)</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">24-30 months’</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">27 months’</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$221,172.91</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">29 months’ (at least)</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">24-30 months’</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">30 months’</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$227,403.66</p></td><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">30 months’ (at least)</p></td><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">24-30 months’</p></td><td align="left" class="" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">30 months’</p></td></tr></tbody></table>

  
  

354    In calibrating the sentence for the charge involving $127,104.40 (the lowest quantum), I considered _Xie Ming Liang_. This was because two of the proceeded charges there involved comparable sums ($118,751.20 and $138,000). They were also punishable under s 408 of the Penal Code.[^552] In addition, the offences there were premeditated and the offender was not considered a first-time offender since he had multiple charges.

355    That said:

> (a)     while the offender in _Xie Ming Liang_ had displayed a “total lack of contrition” when he “took advantage of \[the Managing Director’s\] generosity \[in giving him a second chance\] and carried on committing further offences, he _did_ plead guilty. Coupled with his restitution (albeit of a “hardly…appreciable amount”), this demonstrated at least _some_ remorse on his part. His guilty plea also dispensed with the need for witnesses to testify and saved judicial resources.[^553] Here, Quah was convicted only after a 10-day trial, in which she showed no remorse.

> (b)     the quality and degree of trust the Company/Directors reposed in Quah was _greater_ than that reposed in the offender in _Xie Ming Liang_.

> (c)     the offender in _Xie Ming Liang_ did not take any steps to conceal his misappropriation of the company’s monies, unlike Quah.

356    However, the four similar charges considered in sentencing in _Xie Ming Liang_ would have increased the sentences imposed in respect of the three proceeded charges.[^554] A term of two years’ imprisonment was _also_ imposed for the charge involving $138,000 – this was about $11,000 more than the sum of $127,104.40 in Quah’s charge.

357    All things considered, I thought that an appropriate sentence for the charge involving $127,104.40 was about **27 months’ imprisonment**.

358    The charges involving $169,974.60 and $174,166.12 merited higher sentences. These sums exceeded $127,104.40 by more than $40,000. An appropriate sentence for these charges was about **29 months’ imprisonment**.

359    The remaining two charges involved sums of $221,172.91 and $227,403.66. In calibrating the sentences for these charges, I considered _Destry Tan_. There, 24 months’ imprisonment was imposed for a charge involving a _smaller_ sum of $214,139.20. Like the instant case, the offences were premeditated and the offender was in an elevated position of trust. How Quah used the misappropriated monies was, however, unclear. The Clients’ operations also appeared unaffected by Quah’s offences. That said, the offender in _Destry Tan_ had pleaded guilty and made _full_ restitution. There was also no evidence that he took steps to conceal his misappropriation of the company’s monies. Indeed, he confessed his misdeeds when confronted. The single charge considered in sentencing would have increased the sentence for the proceeded charge (albeit, minimally, as it amounted to only about 10% of the sum involved for the proceeded charge). All things considered, I thought that an appropriate sentence for these charges was about **32 months’ imprisonment**.

360    The table below lists the individual sentences I thought appropriate:

<table align="left" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="Judg-2-tblr" frame="all" pgwide="1"><colgroup><col width="31.64%"> <col width="68.36%"> </colgroup><tbody><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Sum misappropriated</b></p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Appropriate individual sentences</b></p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$127,104.40</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">27 months’ imprisonment</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$169,974.60</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">29 months’ imprisonment</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$174,166.12</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">29 months’ imprisonment</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$221,172.91</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">32 months’ imprisonment</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$227,403.66</p></td><td align="left" class="" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">32 months’ imprisonment</p></td></tr></tbody></table>

  
  

#### Stage 2: Determining how individual sentences should run

361    The second stage of the analysis is to determine how the individual sentences should run. As a general rule, sentences for unrelated offences should run consecutively.[^555] Here, at least two sentences had to run consecutively given s 307(1) of the CPC. In this regard, Counsel argued that _only_ two sentences should be run consecutively because of the one-transaction rule:

> 22.    In \[_Raveen_\], the Honourable High Court held that the premise of the one-transaction principle is that “_if there is a single invasion of a legally protected interest, then even if this might give rise to several offences, it is, in the final analysis, the violation of that single interest that is being punished and concurrent sentences would thus ordinarily suffice to reflect the seriousness of the offences_.”

> …

> 24.    We submit that all 5 offences pertain to the same “_single invasion of a legally protected interest_”. The latter is reflected in the fact that the victim is the same in all 5 charges, and the nature of the offence is also the same in all 5 charges. As such, the one-transaction principle should apply.

> \[emphasis added in original\]

362    This submission was a misapplication of the one-transaction rule. Where two or more offences are committed in the course of a _single_ transaction, this rule requires that all sentences in respect of those offences should be concurrent rather than consecutive.[^556] It is an evaluative rule directed towards whether an offender should be doubly punished for offences that have been committed simultaneously or close together in time.[^557] In the final analysis, however, its application is an exercise in common sense.[^558]

363    This was not a case where the offences underlying the charges were committed in the course of a _single_ transaction. The “victim” might have been the Company throughout. The nature of the offences might have been the same as well. But, the charges reflected offending over _different_ periods (and not simultaneously). Indeed, each charge encompassed several _distinct_ instances of misappropriation. This was not a case where the offences involved a “single invasion of the same legally protected interest”. Rather, they involved _multiple_ invasions of the same legally protected interest. In these circumstances, it would have been an affront to common sense to suggest that the one-transaction rule applied to limit the number of sentences that should be run consecutively. The net effect of Counsel’s submission was that an offender could _repeatedly_ commit the same type of offence against the same victim on _different_ occasions and still expect a sentencing discount – _regardless_ of how prolonged the offending conduct. This was tantamount to an inducement to criminal conduct.

364    The question of whether Quah’s offences were related or otherwise “should be addressed with due sensitivity to the facts and a healthy dose of common sense”.[^559] The offences underlying her charges were plainly separate and unrelated (in that they were not part of a single transaction). The general rule that sentences for such offences should run consecutively thus applied. The application of this general rule in this case would have yielded 149 months’ imprisonment (or 12 years’ and 5 months’ imprisonment).[^560]

#### Stage 3: Application of totality principle

365    The third stage of the sentencing analysis is to apply the totality principle, and take a “last look” at all the facts and circumstances to ensure that the aggregate sentence is sufficient and proportionate to the offender’s overall criminality.[^561] There are two limbs to this principle, characterised by the following questions:

> (a)     whether the total sentence is substantially above the normal level of sentences for the most serious of the individual offences; and

> (b)     whether the effect of the total sentence on the offender is crushing and not in keeping with her past record and future prospects.

366    A court should bear in mind the following rationales for the principle:[^562]

> (a)     “an aggregation resulting in a longer sentence is going to carry a compounding effect that bears more than a liner relation to the cumulative and overall criminality of the case”.

> (b)     “an extremely long aggregate sentence may induce a feeling of hopelessness that destroys all prospects of the offender’s subsequent rehabilitation and reintegration”.

367    The totality principle should ordinarily apply with greater force in cases involving longer aggregate sentences – for instance, where the offender is sentenced for numerous offences in a single sitting or where the individual sentences are themselves relatively lengthy. The longer the aggregated sentence, the greater the risk of a disproportionate sentence. If an aggregate sentence is considered excessive, a court may opt for a different combination of sentences to run consecutively or adjust the individual sentences.[^563]

368    In this regard, Counsel relied on three cases to argue that only two sentences should be run consecutively – to “result in an aggregate imprisonment term of between 48 to 60 months, _ie_, 4 to 5 years”:[^564]

> (a)      _PP v Yeo Kay Keng Matthew_ (“_Matthew Yeo_”)[^565];

> (b)      _Luciana Lim_; and

> (c)      _PP v Christopher Foong Yew Eong_ (“_Christopher Foong_”).[^566]

369    I did not find Counsel’s submissions persuasive. I now elaborate.

#### Matthew Yeo

370    The offender (who had no antecedents) faced 32 charges.[^567] These included three under s 408 of the Penal Code. He pleaded guilty to nine charges. Two of these nine charges (and the most serious of the proceeded charges) were under s 408 of the Penal Code.[^568] The remaining seven charges were for converting, or removing from Singapore, property representing the offender’s benefits of criminal conduct (obtained from the commission of the offences under s 408 of the Penal Code).

371    I set out the key details of all the three charges under s 408 of the Penal Code:

<table align="left" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="Judg-2-tblr" frame="all" pgwide="1"><colgroup><col width="37.24%"> <col width="48.94%"> <col width="13.82%"> </colgroup><tbody><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Offending period</b></p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Property dishonestly converted</b></p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Remarks</b></p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">April 2008 to March 2009 (period of about 11 months)</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">58 mobile phones, which the offender sold for about $18,000</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">TIC</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">April 2009 to March 2010 (period of about 11 months)</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">826 mobile phones, valued at approximately $500,000</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">Proceed</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">April 2010 to November 2010 (period of about 7 months)</p></td><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">2201 mobile phones, valued at approximately $1.5m</p></td><td align="left" class="" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">Proceed</p></td></tr></tbody></table>

  
  

372    The offender was employed with the local telecommunications company, M1, as its Accounts Manager for Corporate Sales. His duties included the sale of mobile phones and subscription contracts for mobile voice and/or data communications services to corporate customers. Between April 2008 and November 2010, he made use of his position to misappropriate phones belonging to M1. He did this by falsifying subscription contacts purportedly entered into by corporate customers. Under the terms of these contracts, the corporate customers would be entitled to free or subsidised phones. The offender would raise sales orders based on these falsified contracts. New phones would then be issued by M1 against these sales orders. The offender would intercept these phones before they could be delivered by courier to the corporate customers, by claiming that he would personally deliver them. If the phones were to be sold at a subsidised price (as opposed to being free), he would pay the subsidised price.

373    The offender would then convert these phones to his own use by selling them to third parties for cash, at or near their retail prices. To avoid detection, he intercepted and disposed of the falsified contracts before the relevant department in M1 could activate them. In some cases, he would falsify the contracts in a way he knew they would be rejected and returned to him to rectify – and when this happened, he would not follow up. His actions were uncovered during an internal reorganisation in November 2010. When confronted by M1 on 15 November 2010, he made a full confession. M1 then referred the matter to the police.

374    Investigations established that the offender converted 3,085 phones in all, and made approximately $2m from selling them. The estimated total loss to M1 was about $2.059m (being the difference between the retail price of the phones and the subsidised price the offender paid). The offender spent the monies obtained on luxury cars, watches, and bags. Accounting for the recovery of some of these items, the estimated total loss to M1 was at about $1.1m. The offender did not make any restitution for this shortfall.

375    The offender received the _same_ sentence of five years’ imprisonment for both charges under s 408 of the Penal Code:

<table align="left" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="Judg-2-tblr" frame="all" pgwide="1"><colgroup><col width="71.48%"> <col width="28.52%"> </colgroup><tbody><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Property dishonestly converted</b></p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Sentence imposed</b></p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">826 mobile phones, valued at approximately <b>$500,000</b></p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">5 years’ imprisonment</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">2201 mobile phones, valued at approximately <b>$1.5m</b></p></td><td align="left" class="" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">5 years’ imprisonment</p></td></tr></tbody></table>

  
  

376    One of these sentences was ordered to run consecutively with the sentence of one year’s imprisonment for one of the charges for converting property representing the benefits of criminal conduct. The total sentence imposed was hence six years’ imprisonment. The offender appealed against the sentences. His appeal was later deemed withdrawn when no petition of appeal was filed.

377    I found _Matthew Yeo_ to be of limited assistance in calibrating the total sentence in this case. This was for four reasons. First, the sentences imposed in _Matthew Yeo_ were never considered by an appellate court (unlike _Destry Tan_ and _Xie Ming Liang_). Second, a sentencing discount was merited in _Matthew Yeo_ because the offender pleaded guilty.[^569]

378     Third, the monetary loss to M1 (or the harm caused by the offender’s offences) was not $2m but about half of that at $.1.1m. I was aware that the District Court in _Matthew Yeo_ was of the view that “the \[fortuitous\] recovery of the items \[was\] of limited mitigation and clearly not evidence of any contriteness on the part of the \[offender\]”.[^570] It also held the view that such recovery did “not have the same standing as that of an offender making restitution out of \[his\] own volition and remorse even before his misdeeds come to light”.[^571] That said, it _did_ recognise that “the amount recovered does have some mitigating value”.[^572] It would have been wrong, in principle, to punish the offender in _Matthew Yeo_ as if the measure of loss to M1 (and the harm caused) was in fact $2m. After all, the severity of an offence must be assessed by having regard to the principal parameters of harm caused and the offender’s culpability.[^573]_Matthew Yeo_ did not therefore support the simple proposition that the misappropriation of $2m should _ipso facto_ attract five years’ imprisonment for an offence under s 408 of the Penal Code.

379     Fourth, the District Court had imposed the _same_ sentence in respect of _both_ charges under s 408 of the Penal Code – although the stated loss to M1 (_sans_ recovery, the quantum of which was, in any event, unclear with respect to each charge) in relation to one charge ($500,000) was _thrice_ that in relation to the other charge ($1.5m).[^574] In doing so, the court reasoned as follows:

> 49.    In conclusion, I find that a substantial custodial sentence is warranted in the present case and taking into account all the aggravating factors highlighted above and the lack of any strong mitigating circumstances, the appropriate sentence is 6 years. Taking into consideration that I would have to order at least 2 sentences of imprisonment to run consecutively, I impose a period of 5 years for the criminal breach of trust offence\[s\] and 1 year’s imprisonment for the…charges \[under the Corruption, Drug Trafficking and Other Serious Crimes (Confiscation of Benefits) Act\] and order that one sentence from each category to run consecutively totalling 6 years.

380    This global approach to sentencing was consistent with that stated in _PP v Teo Cheng Kiat_.[^575] There, the High Court remarked as follows:

> 3     **The ten Charges in the present case involve sums of money ranging from $988,000 to $6 million.** Like \[_Wong Kai Chuen Phillip v PP_\], the Charges here have been divided into convenient periods of time and relate to different bank accounts. **The individual sums of money involved in each Charge are therefore not quite as important as the total amount of money misappropriated** in all the 25 criminal breach of trust Charges. Similarly, **the individual sentences are not of so much practical significance as the aggregate of the sentences finally imposed.**

> …

> 10    As I have indicated earlier, in a case such as this **where there are multiple Charges, it is of no real practical significance what the individual sentences ought to be. I will not therefore try to tailor each sentence according to the amounts misappropriated**…

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

381    The global approach to sentencing was, however, later rejected by the High Court in _PP v Syamsul Hilal bin Ismail_ (“_Syamsul_”)[^576] for “putting the cart before the horse”:

> 25    Before I move on to explain my perception as to the appropriate sentence for the offences in question, some observations should be made of the…District Judge’s sentencing approach. It was apparent from the GD that the…District Judge first took the provisional view that a global sentence of around nine to ten months’ imprisonment would be appropriate for all the offences which the Respondent had committed and on that basis worked backwards to determine the sentence per charge for each of the three categories of offences proceeded on…Undoubtedly, his provisional view on the global sentence to be imposed on the Respondent had directly influenced his determination of the appropriate sentence for each of the individual offences.

> 26    In my view, the better and more logical approach would have been to first determine the sentences for the individual offences by having regard to the respective sentencing precedents, before stepping back for a holistic perspective in order to determine which sentences should be ordered to run consecutively to make for an appropriate global sentence. This two-stage approach was alluded to by V K Rajah JA in _ADF v PP_ <span class="citation">\[2010\] 1 SLR 874</span> at \[92\]:

>> …Where multiple distinct offences have been committed, sentencing is a two-stage process. First, the sentence for each individual offence had to be determined. Second, the court has to determine whether the sentences for these multiple offences ought to run concurrently or consecutively and if consecutively, which combination of sentences ought to be made and whether the overall sentence properly comprehends the criminality of the multiple offender…

> 27    Keeping the two stages distinct would allow for a more principled approach in determining the appropriate sentence to be imposed for each individual offence than if the court were to determine the same by working backwards from a pre-conceived global sentence. This is because a court would generally not be able to properly comprehend the overall criminality of the multiple offender (so as to determine an appropriate global sentence) unless it first comes to grips with the individual offences by going through the exercise of determining the appropriate sentence for each offence (in this case, due to the large number of charges, beginning by determining an appropriate sentence per charge for each of the categories of offences would have been acceptable), having regard to the applicable sentencing precedents. To begin by first considering and determining the appropriate global sentence would be like putting the cart before the horse.

382    The two-stage approach to sentencing in _Syamsul_ is now established law.[^577]

383    Because the global approach to sentencing was adopted in _Matthew Yeo_, this “directly influenced \[the\] determination of the appropriate sentence for each of the individual offences”.[^578] It was apparent that the court in _Matthew Yeo_ had determined the appropriate sentences to impose for each individual offence by working backwards from its pre-conceived global sentence of six years’ imprisonment. I therefore did not think that the sentences imposed in _Matthew Yeo_ (whether the individual sentences or the total sentence) were reliable indicators to calibrate the sentences in this case.

#### Luciana Lim

384    The offender (who had no antecedents) faced 11 charges.[^579] These included two under s 408 of the Penal Code. She pleaded guilty to four charges. The most serious of these four charges was one of the charges under s 408 of the Penal Code. This involved her dishonest conversion of wines and spirits worth a total of $6,370,280.48 between July 2012 and June 2013. The wines and spirits belonged to a company (dealing in such products) that employed her as a relationship manager.[^580] She had placed numerous fraudulent orders for expensive alcohol on credit under the names of fictitious clients during this six-month period. She then sold the alcohol (with a retail value of $6,370,280.48) and retained the proceeds. The _unusual_ facts in _Luciana Lim_ were summarised by the High Court in _Kavitha_, as it explained why the culpability of the offenders in both cases was _less_ than the “median criminal breach of trust offender”:

> 24    …_Luciana Lim_…concerned criminal breach of trust by a clerk or servant **who committed the offence due to harassment by unlicensed moneylenders rather than for pecuniary gain**…**Crucially, she derived no pecuniary benefit from the offence. She used the moneys to pay debts owed to illegal moneylenders, and committed the offences while labouring under significant pressure due to harassment by them. \[T\]he offender was not herself the borrower from the illegal moneylenders, but was a trusting friend who had agreed to stand as guarantor for a loan taken out by a former colleague.** At first instance, the district court held that an appropriate starting point was a nine-year imprisonment term. The offender was given a one-third discount on account of the mitigating factors, **particularly her reason for the commission of the offence and the fact that she had derived no pecuniary benefit from the crime.** Hence, she was sentenced to only six years’ imprisonment for the offence under s 408 of the Penal Code.

> 25    On appeal, the Prosecution argued that the district court had given undue weight to the offender’s absence of personal greed as a mitigating factor. **Applying the principles set out above on the role of motive in sentencing, See JC held that an offender who** **_does not_** **commit criminal breach of trust for personal gain is less culpable, relatively speaking, than the median criminal breach of trust offender (at \[56\]). Hence, he agreed with the district court’s assessment that the case was unusual and that the sentence could not readily be benchmarked against cases where the offender committed the crime for personal gain.** The district court was entitled to find that the offender’s culpability was significantly reduced and to afford her an appropriate sentencing discount (at \[63\]).

> 26    Applying the principles set out in _Luciana Lim_, with which I agree, it was apparent that the appellant’s reason for committing the offence in this case, which was her fear arising from the threats made by the unlicensed moneylender, particularly in relation to her young son, was a less culpable motive, which warranted the imposition of a lower sentence…To put it another way, **the appellant was clearly less culpable than the offenders in the sentencing precedents which the prosecution had relied on. There was no indication that the offences in those cases had been committed for reasons other than greed and avarice. Thus it would be wrong to benchmark the appellant’s sentence against those precedents.**

> \[emphasis in original; emphasis added in bold\]

385    In _Luciana Lim_, the High Court also gave weight to the “cooperation \[the offender\] rendered the authorities in the course of the investigations”[^581]:

> 73    …On the one hand, I was mindful that the harm caused by the accused far exceeds that in any of the four cases I have referred to because of the sheer quantum of the loss sustained by \[the company\]. **On the other hand, however, the accused was far less culpable than any of the aforementioned offenders, all of whom were motivated by avarice and many of whom led lavish lifestyles with their ill-gotten gains before they were prosecuted. There were many unique features of this case which called out for special attention, such as the absence of a desire for personal gain, the pressure placed on the accused by the harassment, and the co-operation she rendered the authorities in the course of the investigations.** When I considered the facts in their totality, it seemed to me that the sentence of six years’ imprisonment imposed by the District Judge in respect of the CBT charge and the global sentence of seven years’ imprisonment was neither manifestly excessive nor manifestly inadequate.

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

386    Counsel argued that Quah should be sentenced to a total “imprisonment term of between 48 to 60 months, _ie_, 4 to 5 years \[only\]” because:[^582]

> (a)     unlike the offender in _Luciana Lim_ who received six years’ imprisonment for misappropriating nearly $6.4m, Quah had misappropriated less than $1m.

> (b)     the High Court in _Luciana Lim_ had recognised that between five to six years’ imprisonment should be imposed where between $1m and $2m are misappropriated, and Quah had misappropriated less than $1m.

387     _Luciana Lim_ supported neither of these points. First, it was clear that the sentence of six years’ imprisonment in _Luciana Lim_ was because of its “unique features” which made the offender “far less culpable” than the “median criminal breach of trust offender”.[^583] These features were absent in this case. In addition, unlike the instant case, there was no evidence in _Luciana Lim_ that the offender (who had also pleaded guilty):[^584]

> (a)     had been “entrusted with any special level of responsibility”; and

> (b)     “took any particular steps to avoid detection”.

388     Second, the High Court in _Luciana Lim_ did _not_ in fact recognise that between five to six years’ imprisonment should be imposed where between $1m and $2m are misappropriated. The court was simply setting out the Prosecution’s sentencing position based on three cases it had tendered – namely, two unreported cases and _Matthew Yeo_. Indeed, the court declined to “derive a sentencing range \[for the quantum involved in _Luciana Lim_\] from such a small sample size (particularly considering the circumspection with which unreported decisions are generally viewed)”. In addition, the High Court referred to _Matthew Yeo_ only because it was comparable in “terms of _modus operandi_ and level of culpability”. At no point did it endorse any sentencing position based on the misappropriated quantum _alone_. I reproduce the relevant paragraphs in _Luciana Lim_:

> 68    As far as the precedents were concerned, the Prosecution cautioned against reliance on the older cases and submitted that there had been a “clear upward trend in the sentences for such offences in recent years”. **They drew my attention to the following three decisions of the District Court –** **_PP v Lee Han Boon Adrian_** **DAC 911016/2014, P****_P v Koh Mui Hoong_** **DAC 500714/2013**, **_PP v Yeo Kay Keng Matthew_** **<span class="citation">\[2011\] SGDC 425</span> (“****_Matthew Yeo_****”) – all of which involved the misappropriation of between $1m and $2m and in respect of which sentences of between five and six years’ imprisonment were imposed.** Extrapolating from this, they submitted that the starting point of nine years’ imprisonment arrived at by the District Judge was too low and that a starting point of between ten and 12 years’ imprisonment ought to have been ordered instead. With respect, I could not agree with this.

> 69    There is no question that the amount of money misappropriated is an important sentencing consideration. Generally, the larger the amount misappropriated, the more severe the punishment that should be meted out. However, it has been emphasised in past cases that while the punishments imposed for CBT offences ought to increase in severity with the amounts misappropriated, the sentences “do not bear a relationship of linear proportionality with the sums involved” (see _PP v Tan Cheng Yew_ <span class="citation">\[2013\] 1 SLR 1095</span> (“_Tan Cheng Yew_”) at \[184\]). The fact that proportionately higher punishments were imposed for offences involving smaller sums of money does not, without more, imply that the District Judge had selected an inappropriate starting point or that the eventual sentence she arrived at was manifestly inadequate. **I also did not think it possible to derive a sentencing range from such a small sample size (particularly considering the circumspection with which unreported decisions are generally viewed**: see _Luong Thi Trang Hoang Kathleen v PP_ <span class="citation">\[2010\] 1 SLR 707</span> at \[21\]).

> 70     **When I turned to consider the precedents cited, there were four which I found particularly helpful. In terms of** **_modus operandi_** **and level of culpability, I considered** **_Matthew Yeo_** **to be comparable**….

> 71     **In terms of the quantum**, the closest precedents which were cited were the cases of _Joachim Kang Hock Chai v PP_ DAC 156/2003 and _PP v Kwek Chee Tong_ <span class="citation">\[2001\] SGDC 194</span>….

> 72    I also had regard to the case of _Tan Cheng Yew_ which, though far more aggravated in many respects, also provided a useful point of reference \[in terms of quantum\]…

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

#### Christopher Foong

389    The offender (who had no antecedents) was an “administrative assistant” in a company. He was its only full-time employee, and there was no one to check on his work. Apart from the offender, the company employed an accountant on a part-time basis. The director of the company was one Budi Agusalim, an Indonesian national.

390    The offender was given electronic banking access to the company’s three bank accounts with UOB, to check the balances in these accounts.

391    While employed by the company, he incorporated a company (“Paminco”) in Malaysia. Paminco never had any dealings with the company.

392    Sometime in 2006, Budi needed the bank accounts statements of the company to put up its financial report. The offender failed to give these to Budi, and made excuses for not being able to do so. Budi eventually called UOB sometime in August 2006, and sought the bank statements for the past 2 months. On receiving these statements, he discovered that fraudulent transactions had been made _via_ internet banking to Paminco.

393    On 24 August 2006, Budi’s son lodged a police report alleging that the offender has misappropriated monies belonging to the company. When Budi’s son attended at the company’s office the following day, he saw the resignation letter of the offender (dated 15 August 2006), with the offender’s salary of $2,000 enclosed (in cash). Investigations showed that the offender had left Singapore for Malaysia on 19 August 2006.

394    The offender was arrested when he returned to Singapore on 6 February 2013. During investigations, he lied that Budi had instructed him to incorporate Paminco and to transfer not more than USD$50,000 per month to it (apparently because Budi needed to “park” monies in Malaysia in case Singapore, which was in elections mode at that time, changed its policy concerning trading in Singapore). The offender eventually admitted that he had transferred monies from the company’s bank accounts to Paminco to help with his “personal expenses in Malaysia” when he left “Singapore into Malaysia”.

395    The offender faced five charges, for misappropriating a total of $1,321,050.25 between 3 March 2004 and 18 August 2006. No restitution was made. He pleaded guilty to three charges. The two remaining two charges were considered in sentencing. The charges were _all_ punishable under s 408 of the 1985 Penal Code, with imprisonment not exceeding seven years.

396    The key details of all five charges are set out in the table below:

![]([2020] SGDC 0066_Image/[2020] SGDC 0066_image5.png)

397    The District Court reasoned as follows before sentencing the offender:

> 4     **The prescribed punishment under s 408 of the Penal Code at the time the offences were committed was imprisonment of up to 7 years and/or to a fine.**

> 5    Several aggravating factor were present:-

>> (a)    The amount involved was large. For the proceeded charges, the amount involved was about $1,197,000. When the TIC charges were included, the total amount involved was about $1,321,000.

>> (b)    The trust reposed was high. The accused was the sole full-time employee of the company. There were few (if any) checks on him. He had full electronic access to the company’s accounts with UOB bank. He took advantage of the trust placed in him to commit the offences.

>> (c)    The offences were committed over an extended period of about two and a half years. The first offence was committed in March 2004 while the final offence (which was for the largest sum) was committed in August 2006. Shortly after the commission of the last offence, the accused absconded to Malaysia.

>> (d)    There was planning and pre-meditation. The accused had incorporated a company with his wife in Malaysia and opened a bank account for it. Thereafter, he transferred the company’s money to the Malaysian company’s bank account without authorisation or consent.

>> (e) No restitution was made.

> 6    In sentencing the accused, I also took into account the mitigating factors in his favour. He had no antecedents. **He had pleaded guilty, which was an indication of his remorse. He informed the court that he had surrendered 5 years ago and had since been waiting to plead guilty.** He also stated that he co-operated with investigations and provided information to the best of his recollection. It was also apparent that the accused had health issues as he had diabetes and gout.

> …

> 13    As the amount which the accused had misappropriated was large, sentence should correspondingly be high. Many aggravating factors were also present. **I was however of the view that the prosecution’s submission for a total of 8 years’ imprisonment was too high. The offences were also committed between 2004 and 2006.** Having considered the accused’s culpability, the harm caused and the aggravating and mitigating factors, I was of the view that for the 2nd and 3rd charges (which involved about SGD 273,000 and SGD 276,000) the appropriate sentence would be 2 years’ imprisonment for each charge. For the 5th charge (which involved about SGD 638,000), I was of the view that a term of 4 years’ imprisonment was appropriate.

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

398    The court imposed the following sentences:

![]([2020] SGDC 0066_Image/[2020] SGDC 0066_image6.png)

399    The Prosecution discontinued its appeal against the sentences imposed.[^585]

400    I did not find Counsel’s submissions[^586] persuasive for three reasons. First, the charges in _Christopher Foong_ were all punishable under s 408 of the 1985 Penal Code – with up to seven years’ imprisonment (only). The charges in the instant case were all punishable under s 408 of the Penal Code – with up to 15 years’ imprisonment. This was clearly a factor that weighed heavily in the decision of the District Court (and rightfully so[^587]), when it differed from the Prosecution’s submissions for a total sentence of at least 8 years’ imprisonment – and imposed a total sentence of 6 years’ imprisonment instead.[^588]

401     Second, it appears that the District Court also gave significant weight to the offender having returned to Singapore to surrender some six years after he fled to Malaysia.[^589] This would possibly explain why the individual sentences and the total sentence in _Christopher Foong_ were identical to those imposed in in _Choo Mui v PP_[^590], which involved fewer charges and a smaller quantum.

402    The offender in _Choo Mui_ was a secretary in a company. She was entrusted with its chequebooks, and authorised to issue cheques to pay its suppliers. Between January 1997 and April 1998 (a period of 15 months), she credited these cheques into her personal bank account – misappropriating a total of $677,837.63 in the process (_ie_, about half the total sum misappropriated in _Christopher Foong_). She then failed to turn up for work one day, having taken the company’s accounts and documents with her. She left only a resignation letter. None of the misappropriated monies were recovered. The offender also did not make any restitution. She spent the monies on her personal expenses. She also used the monies to invest in shares and for loans to friends. At the time she was sentenced, she was married with a six-year-old child and expecting another child. She had no prior antecedents. She pleaded guilty to two charges under s 408 of the 1985 Penal Code, and was sentenced as follows:

<table align="left" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="Judg-2-tblr" frame="all" pgwide="1"><colgroup><col width="30.5338932213557%"> <col width="30.2139572085583%"> <col width="39.252149570086%"> </colgroup><tbody><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Sum misappropriated</b></p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Sentence</b></p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Remarks</b></p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$467,877.63</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">4 years’ imprisonment</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">Consecutive</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$209,960</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">2 years’ imprisonment</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">Consecutive</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1"><b>$677,837.63</b></p></td><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Total sum/sentence</b></p></td><td align="left" class="" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1"><b>6 years’ imprisonment</b></p></td></tr></tbody></table>

  
  

403    The High Court dismissed the offender’s appeal against sentence.[^591]

404     Third, the sentences imposed in _Christopher Foong_ were never considered by an appellate court (unlike _Destry Tan_ and _Xie Ming Liang_).

##### Conclusion on total sentence

405    Had I ordered all the individual sentences imposed to run consecutively, this would have yielded a total of 149 months’ imprisonment:

<table align="left" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="Judg-2-tblr" frame="all" pgwide="1"><colgroup><col width="29.78%"> <col width="70.22%"> </colgroup><tbody><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Sum misappropriated</b></p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Appropriate individual sentences</b></p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$127,104.40</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">27 months’ imprisonment</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$169,974.60</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">29 months’ imprisonment</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$174,166.12</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">29 months’ imprisonment</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$221,172.91</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">32 months’ imprisonment</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$227,403.66</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">32 months’ imprisonment</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Total sentence</b></p></td><td align="left" class="" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1"><b>149 months’ imprisonment</b></p></td></tr></tbody></table>

  
  

406    A total sentence of 149 months’ imprisonment would have been equivalent to a term of 12 years and 5 months. Such a sentence would clearly be substantially above the range of sentences normally imposed for offences under s 408 of the Penal Code.[^592] It would still fall short of the maximum prescribed punishment of 15 years’ imprisonment for an offence under s 408 of the Penal Code. That said, its effect on Quah would likely be crushing and destroy any reasonable expectation of useful life after release. In this regard, I was mindful that Quah was 65 years old[^593] when she was sentenced – and the average life expectancy for females in Singapore was about 85 years as of 2018.[^594] A total sentence of 149 months’ imprisonment was very likely to induce in her a feeling of hopelessness, and destroy all prospects of her subsequent rehabilitation and reintegration.

407    However, I was equally mindful that Quah’s relatively advanced age was also a consequence of the period of time during which she was able to keep her misdeeds concealed. I therefore did not think that her age alone was good enough reason to order only two sentences to run consecutively. This was in light of the overall criminality of her conduct – which was especially evident when the five charges against her were dissected. Each charge did not encompass just one instance of misappropriation, but _multiple_ instances of misappropriation of _distinct_ payments (that she collected from the Clients on different occasions).

408    The many instances of misappropriation encompassed by each of the charges against Quah are set out in the table below.[^595] The figures show that, on average, Quah misappropriated between two to three payments each month, for over nearly five years:

![]([2020] SGDC 0066_Image/[2020] SGDC 0066_image7.png)

409    Her persistent offending (accompanied each time with the manipulation of the Company’s accounting records to hide her misappropriation) called for more than two sentences to run consecutively.[^596] It reflected a more directed criminal intent, and a greater commitment to offending. It heightened her culpability, and showed her total disregard for the law.

410    Having regard to the totality principle, I adjusted the individual sentences downwards; and ordered that only three of these sentences – the longest sentence; the shortest sentence; and the sentence in-between – be run consecutively:

<table align="left" cellpadding="0" cellspacing="0" class="Judg-2-tblr" frame="all" pgwide="1"><colgroup><col width="23.82%"> <col width="42.54%"> <col width="33.64%"> </colgroup><tbody><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Misappropriated</b></p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Appropriate individual sentences</b></p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="center" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Adjusted sentences</b></p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$127,104.40</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">27 months’ imprisonment</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">24 months’ imprisonment</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$169,974.60</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">29 months’ imprisonment</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">27 months’ imprisonment</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$174,166.12</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">29 months’ imprisonment</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">27 months’ imprisonment</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$221,172.91</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">32 months’ imprisonment</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">30 months’ imprisonment</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">$227,403.66</p></td><td align="left" class="br" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">32 months’ imprisonment</p></td><td align="left" class="b" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">30 months’ imprisonment</p></td></tr><tr><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1"><b>Total sentence</b></p></td><td align="left" class="r" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1">149 months’ imprisonment</p></td><td align="left" class="" rowspan="1" valign="top"><p align="justify" class="Table-Para-1"><b>81 months’ imprisonment</b></p></td></tr></tbody></table>

  
  

411    The aggregate sentence of 81 months’ imprisonment was equivalent to a term of 6 years’ and 9 months’ imprisonment. This was a proportionate sentence, which best reflected the overall criminality of Quah’s prolonged offending over almost five years – during which she misappropriated more than $919,000. With remission, Quah would likely be released when she is about 70 years old. The total sentence cannot be said to be _manifestly_ excessive, thus requiring substantial alterations to remedy any injustice.[^597]

**Annex A: Quah’s belated testimony on “Cash Withdrawal Practice**” **[^598]**

> Q:    Now, Mdm Quah, \[your Compilation\] contains withdrawals and transactions marked \[funds transfer\] from three of your personal bank accounts….I’m just asking you to confirm, Mdm Quah, we’ve been through this a number of times. \[Your Compilation\] is just a summary of withdrawals and \[funds transfers\] from your \[Cashline Account; Savings Account; and CashPlus Account\].

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **And you accept that \[your Compilation\] is not a tabulation of all cash withdrawals made from these three \[personal\] accounts during the material period? ...In other words, there are cash withdrawals from these 3 bank accounts that are not included in \[your Compilation\]?**

> **A:**      **I agree, yes.**

> Q:    If you could turn with me to AB2 at page 208.

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    So, this is for your \[Cashline Account\]. And you can see with me, on page 208, that there is a cash withdrawal at ATM for $1,000 on the 5th of September 2010. Do you see that, Mdm Quah?

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **And if you look back at \[your Compilation\]…and I want you to focus your attention on the right most column \[of the 1st page of the 1st sheet\]. Now, this cash withdrawal for $1,000 on 5th of September 2010 it does not appear in \[your Compilation\], you agree?...You…agree with…me it’s not there?**

> **A:**      **\[M\]aybe the date is wrong.**

> **Q:**     Now, Mdm Quah, we went through very extensively during your Examination-in-Chief, the contents of \[your Compilation\]. And in fact, \[your Compilation\] was the amended, agreed, accurate version. So, the question now is very simple, 5th of September 2010, the bank account statement reflects cash withdrawal at ATM of $1,000. This transaction does not appear in \[your Compilation\]? And to help you, Mdm Quah, perhaps we can start on the 1st page of the first sheet at the line item dated, 2nd September 2010. Okay. So, you see that it goes from 2nd September 2010, the one…after that is 7th September 2010, 15th September 2010, 28 September 2010. And then if you flip over the page, 29th September 2010, and then it goes into October 2010 transactions. **So, it’s very clear, you agree, 5th September 2010, cash withdrawal at ATM of $1,000 does not appear in \[your Compilation\]?**

> **A:**      **I think the entry for the 2nd of September 2010 should be the 5th.**

> Q:    Now, Mdm Quah, I’m just going to ask you one more time. Face value document, there is no line item marked 5th September 2010, you agree?

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **So, you’re now telling me that actually, it’s a mistake?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> Q:    And before you go further, I’m going to explain to you why it’s not a mistake. So, if you go with me to page 206 of AB2? Now, if you look at page \[206\]…\[t\]here is, in fact, a cash withdrawal at ATM for $1,000 on 2nd September 2010?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    And that corresponds with \[your Compilation\]?

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **So, it’s not a mistake?**

> **A:**      **It’s not.**

> **Q:**      **But are you now suggesting to me that the 5th September 2010 cash withdrawal was actually meant to be included in \[your Compilation\]?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **So, you are now telling me, suddenly, that it’s $451,400 plus $1,000?**

> **A:**      **Yes**, but I do not want too much trouble. Because I have already prepared this, there’s nothing I can do.

> Q:    So, you are now telling me that you suddenly remembered today, that the $1,000 cash withdrawal on 5th September 2010 is related to the cash cheques from \[EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex\]?

> A:    To put it simply, \[my personal bank account statements were\] given to me. Okay, when I got all these bank documents, I would need to look at them one by one. I would definitely have missed out something. I do not want the trouble, whatever I have put in here it will be based on this. There, I have missed out, we can ignore that. Can we do that?

> **Q:**     Mdm Quah, I think you shouldn’t be asking me questions but I’m just going to summarise. **So, you are telling me that the cash withdrawal on 5th September 2010 is related to the cash cheques \[from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex\]?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **So, you may have handed it to \[TSM\]?**

> **A:**      **Definitely.**

> Q:    But because it was very difficult to analyse \[your\] bank account statements, which we disclosed to you…you had suddenly remembered that actually this $1,000 by right…should \[have been\] included in \[your Compilation\]?

> …

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    So, let’s look at another example. At AB2 again, let’s go to page 212. You can let me know when you are there, Mdm Quah.

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    Now you see that there are cash withdrawals at ATM on 29th October 2010 and 30th October 2010. You see that? For 29th October 2010, it’s a sum of $200 and for 30th October 2010, it’s a sum of $400?

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **And these transactions are not reflected in \[your Compilation\], you agree?...**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **So these cash withdrawals were possibly for \[your\] personal expenses, you agree?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **So, in fact, you accept that these 3 \[personal\] bank accounts in \[your Compilation\] you also withdrew cash for personal expenses?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **So, in reality, for the transaction that we went through before this, the transaction on 5th September 2010 for $1,000, it is completely possible that you withdrew that money for \[your\] personal expenses?**

> **A:**      **No.**

> **Q:**      **You have no way of telling whether this \[withdrawal of $1,000 on 5th September 2010\] was related to the cash cheques \[you collected from EDS, Win Tran, and Time-Flex\] or not?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **You have no system?**

> **A:**      **If I have had a system, I wouldn’t have been cheated by others.**

> **Q:**      **Mdm Quah, I don’t think this is a trial about you being cheated by others but, again, I’m going to ask you to agree. You have no system of telling which cash withdrawals are for handing money to \[TSM\] and which are for personal expenses?**

> **A:**      **Whatever I have handed over to \[TSM\] would usually be in 500 or 1,000 dollars---or 800. My own personal usage---for my own personal usage, I would only withdraw 200 or 400 in case, I needed to buy something.**

> Q:    You said, “Usually.” So clearly, there are exceptions?

> A:    Okay, for those that I withdrew for my own expenses, I would withdr\[a\]w less than 500. Because I needed to know for myself that…every time, whenever I gave to \[TSM\] would be in 500 or 1,000. For the last…3 transactions, because he wanted to…withdraw all the money, so---because at that time, I have already resigned. He wants me to give back the whole amount in \[my personal bank accounts\] to him. So for \[the Cashline Account\] in August 2014, you would see 3 transactions of 800 each.

> **Q:**      **So now, your explanation is that for withdrawals of 500, 800 and 1,000, it’s not that you usually give it to \[TSM\], now it’s always?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **So, I’m giving you a chance to finalise your evidence, because earlier you said, “Usually.”**

> **A:**      **Every time. I want to use that as a mark---the 500 and---1,000…dollars.**

> **Q:**      **And this is the---first---time you are giving this explanation that you know that it’s for \[TSM\].**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **…You didn’t explain this in your Examination-in-Chief even though you would agree it’s quite an important point.**

> **A:**      **I don’t know.**

> Q:    Because on \[your\] bank statements, cash withdrawals just appear as “Cash withdrawals” at ATM?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    And as I have pointed out to you earlier, there are examples, the 5th September 2010 one of the withdrawals of $1,000 that \[was\] not in \[your Compilation\]?

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **So, in reality, Mdm Quah, actually, you have no way of knowing whether the cash withdrawals are for your personal expenses or for handing to \[TSM\]?**

> **A:**      **I have already stated that in every bank, whether it’s UOB or DBS, I would just use 500 and 1,000 as a mark to indicate that I have handed…these \[sums\] to TSM. To me, it’s more convenient.**

> Q:    Okay.

> A:    \[F\]or my personal expenses, I would withdraw less than 500.

> Q:    So, it’s now a convenience issue…

> A:    What do you mean?

> **Q:**      **You chose to withdraw $500 and $1,000 because according to you, it was convenient for you?**

> **A:**      **It is more convenient for me because when I have \[these sums\] to \[TSM\], I did not calculate. I just give them to him.**

> **Q:**      **So, you accept that no matter what the sum in the cash cheque \[you\] deposit\[ed\] into your account, you could choose how much to withdraw?**

> **A:**      **I agree but every time when I wanted to deposit the \[cash\] cheque, I would sit down with \[TSM\] to discuss. He said since you did not want to go to the bank counters to get the money, it’s very troublesome. You could---every day after the cheque was banked in, I could start to get money…for him…from the bank account 2:00pm the next day.**

> **Q:**      **Now, Mdm Quah, you claimed that all these meetings happened with \[TSM\], you have offered no explanation as to who authorised you to only withdraw 500 or 1,000 dollars?**

> **A:**      **It was \[TSM\] who wanted me to do this.**

> **Q:**      **First time you are saying this?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> Q:    So, now again, on the 2nd day of your Cross-Examination, you are raising another new fact which is you had meetings with \[TSM where\]…he told you to deposit cash cheques into your personal bank account and no matter what the sum was, only withdraw in denominations of 500, 1,000 or maybe sometimes 800.

> A:    $800 was only for the last year, 2014.

> **Q:**      **Fine, we’ll confine it to 500 or 1,000 dollars. So, you are now saying for the first time, that it was \[TSM\] who told you to withdraw in denominations of 500 and $1,000. This is the first time you are raising this, do you agree or do you disagree, Mdm Quah?**

> **A:**      **I agree.**

> **Q:**      **This was not put to \[TSM\] even though he came to Court to give evidence, you agree or disagree?**

> **A:**      **Yes, I agree.**

> **Q:**      **So, what you are saying is that even if the cash cheque was for a very specific sum of money with decimal points…you could choose what to withdraw?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **You could let the rest of the money mix in your personal bank accounts. You agree?**

> **A:**      **I agree** but when we do the accounts, usually the cash cheques given to us by our customers, there will be dollars and cents. We’ll round the figure up. For example…if it’s $549, we will round up to 550. We would not include the remaining. Even though that will be included in my bank account…**That was why it was agreed between \[TSM\] and myself that it would be easier to see if it’s 500 or 1,000 because he would look at my bank statements every month, every year.**

> **Q:**      **Okay, So, Mdm Quah, again, I have given you quite a lot of latitude to explain yourself. And you’ve raised a lot of new facts. You agree with me?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **So, now it’s not just that you met with \[TSM\], it’s that \[TSM\] checked your bank account statements?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **And \[TSM\] was the one who told you only withdraw…500 and 1,000?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **So you can let the cash cheque balance, however much it may be, mix with your personal funds in your bank accounts?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **You can apply it to your personal expenses? You can apply it to your personal expenses, yes or no?**

> **A:**      **I agree but---**

> **Q:**      **You agree? That was the question. And you know that it’s actually company money, agree?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> Q:    So, if you spend company money on your personal expenses, that’s actually dishonest?

> A:    Is there such a possibility?

> Q:    Simple proposition, Mdm Quah. If you use company money on your personal expenses, that’s dishonest.

> A:    I have already said---

> Q:    Yes or no, Mdm Quah. Using company money for personal expenses, is it dishonest?

> A:    No, I disagree.

> …

> **Q:**      **So let me re-summarise the proposition for you. Are you trying to tell me that it’s not dishonest if \[TSM\] gave you permission?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **But if \[TSM\] did not give you permission, it would be dishonest, you agree?**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **So, whether \[TSM\] gave you permission is, in fact, a very important fact for your defence.**

> **A:**      **Yes.**

> **Q:**      **And in spite of that, you have raised all these new facts that were not put to \[TSM\].**

> **A:**      **Nobody asked me**.

> Q:    Mdm Quah, you have been assisted by counsel and you have been given adequate opportunity to cross-examine \[TSM\]. So, I’m not going to waste too much time on that point…

**Annex B: Quah’s testimony on cashier’s order**

> Q:    Now, Mdm Quah, earlier in Cross-Examination, the \[Prosecution\] had put it to you that it was illogical for you to withdraw the cashier’s order of $50,000 from your joint account just to transfer to \[your Savings Account\]. You disagreed and then you said that you had your reasons. Do you remember that?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    Okay. Can you now inform the Court what are your reasons?

> A:    \[The account\] is a joint account between me and Johnny Quah. Johnny Quah is my nephew. Before his father passed away, \[his father\] had a sum of money with me…So out of this 50,000 \[that I lent TSM\] there was this 40,000 that…belonged to Johnny Quah. His father wanted him to study. And this money \[was\] meant for his studies. He was studying in Ngee Ann Poly. His school fees would be deducted from the \[Joint Account\] by GIRO. The reason I took this 50,000 out of the \[Joint Account\] was because I did not want to implicate \[my nephew\]. I transferred the money by way of cashier’s order to \[my Savings Account\]. And I wanted \[my nephew\] to open another OCBC account. I know that it is wrong to do this transferring. Because this 40,000 was meant for Johnny’s studies.

> Q:    But Mdm Quah, you said that you didn’t want to implicate Johnny, what is it that you did not want to implicate him in?

> A:    When this 50,000 was deposited into \[my Joint Account\], I told \[TSM\] that 40,000 of this money did not belong to me. It was what I \[had\] borrowed \[from my nephew\]. And then \[TSM\] said, “You can…go to the bank and withdraw 50,000.” And return to \[TSM\] and then he would issue a cheque but why would I want this? Because I did not want to work anymore. I have already tendered by resignation. I have been…asking \[TSM\] to pay me this 50,000 but he refused to do so. He said, “You can take Eric Ho’s cheques to bank into \[your Joint Account\]. I told him, “By doing this, you would implicate me and my nephew. If there is a CBT case, then we would be in big trouble.” He said, “No.” He said that, “You…can bring the…cashier’s order back to office and I will sign for you. And then you don’t owe me anything, I don’t owe you anything.” He signed on the photocopy of this cashier’s order and he took it. But in my computer, I have written down…the circumstances in which he gave me the money. And I have also scanned the cashier’s order. But I could not get…\[TSM’s\] signature. Because he wrote in Chinese on the \[photocopy of\] the cashier’s order…and I translated it for him. He wrote in Chinese and I wrote in English. Because \[it was simply\] phrased. “I do not owe you and you do not owe me.” He took the piece of paper but I have a scan of \[the\] cashier’s order in my computer. There is evidence.

> Q:    Okay. Mdm Quah, is there anything more that you wish to clarify regarding this cashier’s order?

> A:    Initially I wanted cash from him but it’s very dangerous. So…the people from OCBC told me that they would give me a cashier’s order. Because if I were to hand over cash of 50,000 to \[TSM\[, I would still need to go back to the office. Would I be able to get my own money if I hand him the 50,000 in cash? Would I be able to get the cheque from him? This 40,000 has been long owing. In March or April, I have already tendered my resignation. If this dragged on, I would never be able to get my money.

**Annex C: Breakdown of Entrusted Monies**

**Annex D: TSM’s evidence on Monthly Invoices Spreadsheet**

> Q:    Okay, \[TSM\], let’s be clear. \[Fion\] says that there \[are\] Excel spreadsheets in a blue file and on that Excel spreadsheet, it contains information about the amount of the diesel invoices. Are you saying that no such Excel spreadsheet exists?

> A:    No, there is. But…Fion would prepare them to see which customer owed how much every month and show it to me.

> Q:    Okay. So, every month, you are keeping track of which customers have made payment or not, right?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    So, at the end of each month, you would have known…which customers have made payment and which have not, right?

> A:    Yes.

> …

> Q:    Okay, but \[TSM\], you keep track of customer’s payments each month, right?

> A:    I just look at who owe money and was the money paid.

> Q:    So, how do you verify whether the customer actually made payment or not?

> A:    Because every month, it will be printed out from January, February, March and when I see, oh, why this person did not make payment a month, 2 months were owing or 3 months were owing, then I would remind him. So, my responsibility would be to make sure whether this person owed money for a long time and I would go and…remind \[Quah\] to collect payment.

> Q:    Yes, \[TSM\], that is precisely the point, right? You do keep track of which customers have made payment every month.

> A:    Yes, I know whether payment was made but I wouldn’t know whether it would be in cash or in cheque.

> **Q:**      **Okay. So you are saying that you will know whether the customer has made payment or not, right?**

> **A:**      **No, if \[Quah\] cancelled it, that means payment was made. If \[Quah\] did not cancel, that means payment was not made.**

> Q:    So, you were also keeping an eye on customers who have not paid their invoices, right?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    And if from your records, the customer has not paid, you would have reminded \[Quah\] to go and collect payment, right?

> A:    Yes.

> **Q:**      **So, \[TSM\]…I’m going to put it to you that you actually had a system of checking invoices and keeping tracking of payment from customers. Do you agree or disagree?**

> **A:**      **I only take a look at what \[Quah\] showed me.**

> **Q:**      **\[TSM\], the point is that you do keep track, right?**

> **A:**      **If what was given \[to\] me is wrong, I would not know. So, I only looked at what \[Quah\] gave me.**

> …

> Q:    No, what we have is \[Fion\] saying…that she prepared \[the Monthly Invoice Spreadsheets\] for you on a monthly basis, and these spreadsheets allowed you to keep track of which customers made payment or not…So, do you agree with that?

> A:    Every month, they would have to prepare a copy and they would put it there for me to look. **It must have been \[Quah\] who give it to \[Fion\], then she would be able to prepare.**

> Q:    Okay. So, coming back to my question, you have a system of keeping track of payments from customers every month, right?

> A:    Yes.

> Q:    So, you must have known when customers have made payment or when their invoices have become overdue, right?

> A:    By right, I should know.

> …

> **Q:**      **Okay, \[TSM\], that’s the difficulty that we are facing. On one hand, you have accepted that you have a system of keeping track of payment from customers. So, you must have known whether the money actually comes into the company, right? Do you agree?**

> **A:**      **To put it simply, if I can have everything checked so clearly, today I wouldn’t have had money all missing.**

> Q:    \[TSM\], please listen to the question.

> A:    I have already answered the question.

> Q:    Okay, I’ll repeat the question again. You have a system of keeping track of payments from customers. And I put it to you, as a result of that system, you would have also checked whether money actually came into the company.

> A:    I disagree with what you said.

> …

> **Q:**      **And if the customer had made payment by way of a cheque issued to the \[Company’s\] name, you would have checked the \[Company’s Account\] to make sure that the cheque cleared?**

> **A:**      **That’s why I did not check. Otherwise, I wouldn’t have had money missing.**

> …

> **Q:**      **And the fact of the matter was that you were keeping track of customer’s payments and you were keeping track of the monies actually received by the \[Company\]. And that is why you have never \[made\] any complaint to the police or the auditors, you agree?**

> **A:**      **But when I check you must know what I checked. I only checked that to make sure that these customers are not owing money for too long. Because if the debts \[are\] for more than 2 or 3 months then…I must chase after them because my money is to be paid to the diesel company so if I don’t --- if not, I won’t have enough money to make payment. So I cannot allow payment to be dragged on for too long.**

> \[emphasis added in bold\]

[^240]: The Compilation included 17 entries marked “FT”, which referred to “funds transfer” from Quah’s Cashline Account: Day 8, p 51, lines 8-13. In cross-examination, Quah testified that she could only withdraw a maximum of $1,000 a day from her Cashline Account. She said that if she needed to withdraw more than $1,000 on any given day from her Cashline Account, she would have to go to a branch of DBS Bank and produce her ATM card at a counter. According to Quah, DBS Bank would consider such over-the-counter withdrawals as a “funds transfer”: Day 9, pp 46-47. The entries marked “FT” in the Compilation referred to such over-the-counter withdrawals. In chambers, I had asked the Prosecution to verify this with DBS Bank (see also Day 10, p 12). After doing so, the Prosecution did not dispute Quah’s evidence in this regard.

[^510]: \[7\] and \[9\] of the Mitigation Plea & Defence Sentencing Submissions. There also appeared to be some reference to the hardship that would be caused to Quah’s family if she was incarcerated: \[3\]-\[4\] of the Mitigation Plea & Defence Sentencing Submissions (though Counsel did not expressly urge the court to consider such hardship as a mitigating factor. For completeness, I should state that I did consider the relevant paragraphs in the Mitigation Plea & Defence Sentencing Submissions. That said, I attached limited weight to this factor. The cases are clear and consistent that in the absence of very exceptional or extreme circumstances, little, if any, weight should be attached to the fact that an accused’s family will suffer if the accused is imprisoned for a substantial period of time: _PP v Mohammed Liton Mohammed Syeed Mallik_ <span class="citation">\[2008\] 1 SLR(R) 601</span> at \[98\].

[^550]: The District Judge stated in his judgment: ‘The normal sentence for an offence involving more than $500,000 is a sentence of more than 60 months’ imprisonment. In view of the facts and circumstances of the case, especially his full restitution, a one-third reduction from the benchmark was accordingly given. This means the sentence for the accused would have been 40 months’ imprisonment. However, I was of the view than an imprisonment of three years’ would be adequate to serve the ends of justice”: _Sentencing Practice in the Subordinate Courts_ (LexisNexis, 3rd Ed, 2013) at p 798.

I noted that a total 1,102 bottles were recovered from the buyers. I gave weight to her cooperation with authorities...” These observations were not disturbed on appeal.
